[ΕΚΤΟΣ]PREFACE
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The method employed in this edition of the _Monumentum Ancyranum_
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]is suggested by the purpose for which it is intended. That purpose
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]is primarily to adapt it as one of the series of _Translations and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Reprints from the Original Sources of European History_, published
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]by the Department of History of the University of Pennsylvania.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The English version is the core of the work. At the same time the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]opportunity has been seized to present the original texts in such form
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]as to be of real philological service. That there is room for such
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]an edition of the _Monumentum Ancyranum_ there can be no doubt.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The critical edition published by Mommsen in 1883, _Res Gestæ Divi
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Augusti_, must long remain for scholars the sufficient hand-book for
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the study of the greatest of inscriptions. But that edition, with its
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Latin notes, is not adapted for ordinary school or college use, or for
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]historical study by those who do not readily use Latin. And although
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Roman histories constantly refer to this great source for the life and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]times of Augustus, there has been no accessible English translation. It
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]is true that the English translation of Duruy’s _History of Rome_
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]contains a version of the _Monumentum_, but it is not in full
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]accord with the latest text as set forth by Mommsen, and is hidden away
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in the ponderous volumes of that expensive work.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Aside from Mommsen’s edition of 1883, the only recent edition is a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]French one of 1886 by C. Peltier. But this is simply a condensation of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Mommsen. While the present edition depends very largely on Mommsen’s
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]work, it is more than a condensation. Not only is the English version
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]given, but all the known studies of the text published since 1883,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and in criticism of Mommsen, have been collated. The emendations thus
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]suggested have been placed as footnotes to the Latin and Greek texts.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Moreover, the notes have been carefully revised. For the most part they
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]are much reduced in compass, but in many cases they are added to; and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]a large number of typographical errors in Mommsen’s edition have been
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]corrected. Most of these errors were reproduced in the French edition
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]above mentioned. In a work with such a multitude of references it is
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]too much to hope that all errors have been avoided, and the editor will
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]be greatly indebted if users of the book will report them to him.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  W. FAIRLEY.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_University of Pennsylvania, Philadelphia, Pa._
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]INTRODUCTION
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]I. HISTORY OF THE INSCRIPTION.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Suetonius in his _Life of Augustus_ tells us that that Emperor had
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]placed in charge of the Vestal virgins his will and three other sealed
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]documents; and the four papers were produced and read in the senate
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]immediately after his death. One of these additional documents gave
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]directions as to his funeral; another gave a concise account of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]state of the empire; the third contained a list of “his achievements
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]which he desired should be inscribed on brazen tablets and placed
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]before his mausoleum.” These tablets perished in the decline of Rome.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Centuries passed; men had ceased to ask about them, and there was no
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]idea that they would ever be brought to light. Nor were the original
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]tablets ever found. But in 1555 Buysbecche, a Dutch scholar, was sent
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]on an embassy from the Emperor Ferdinand II. to the Sultan Soliman
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]at Amasia in Asia Minor; and a letter of his, published among others
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]at Frankfort in 1595, tells the story of the discovery of a copy of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]this epitaph of Augustus. He writes: “On our nineteenth day from
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Constantinople we reached Ancyra. Here we found a most beautiful
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]inscription, and a copy of those tablets on which Augustus had placed
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the story of his achievements.” From this situation of the copy comes
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the common title, _Monumentum Ancyranum_. Buysbecche made some
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]attempt to copy the Latin inscription, but his work was very hasty and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]incomplete. What he had discovered was of extreme importance, and his
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]report stimulated such interest that European scholars never rested
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]till as complete a copy as possible was finally made in our own time.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The temple on whose walls the inscription was found was one dedicated
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to Augustus and Rome, as was a common custom during the lifetime of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]that Emperor. It was a hexastyle of white marble, with joints of such
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]exquisite workmanship that even in this century it was difficult to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]trace some of them. This temple had served as a Christian church till
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the fifteenth century, and from that time has been part of a Turkish
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]mosque, some sections of its enclosure being used as a cemetery. The
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]great inscription was cut on the two side walls of the pronaos, or
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]vestibule. It was in six pages, three on the left as one entered, and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]three on the right. Each page contained from forty-two to fifty-four
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]lines, and each line an average of sixty letters. The pages cover six
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]courses of the masonry in height, about 2.70 metres, and the length of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the inscription on each wall is about 4 metres. On one of the outer
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]walls of the temple was a Greek translation of the Latin. This measures
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]1.38 metres in height by 21 metres in length. Several Turkish houses
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]had been built against the wall containing this Greek version, and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]this made the reading of it, and still more the copying, an extremely
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]difficult task. The priceless value of the Greek version lies in the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]fact that it supplements in many cases the breaks in the Latin. For
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]it is needless to say that an inscription so old and so exposed has
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]suffered much from time and violence. Various travelers have described
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the temple and its treasure: Tournefort in his _Voyage du Levant_,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Lyons, 1717; Kinneir, _Journey Through Asia Minor_, 1818; Texier,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Description de l’Asie mineure_, Paris, 1839; William Hamilton,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Researches in Asia Minor_, London, 1842; and most completely,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Guillaume, Perrot and Delbet, in their _Exploration archéologique de
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]la Galatie, etc., in 1861_, Paris, 1872.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Numerous attempts were made at transcribing the inscription, and a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]number of editions were published. Buysbecche’s fragments found several
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]editors in the century of their discovery. About a hundred years after
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]him Daniel Cosson, a merchant from Leyden, who had lived many years at
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Smyrna, dying there in 1689, caused an attempt to be made to secure a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]copy, and with somewhat better results. His copy was edited at Leyden
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in 1695. In 1701 Joseph Pitton de Tournefort, under direction of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Louis XIV, visited Ancyra, and attempted to secure a facsimile of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]text. In 1705 Paul Lucas, also sent by Louis XIV, spent twenty days
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in copying the Latin, and his work was the last of its kind till the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]present century. While these early copies are far from being as perfect
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]as more recent ones, they have this value: that in a number of cases
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]they show parts of the inscription which progressive disintegration has
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]now rendered illegible.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The Greek text, owing to the buildings reared against it, was much
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]harder to transcribe. In 1745 Richard Pococke published a few
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]fragments, and in 1832 Hamilton copied pages 10, 11, 12 and 13 of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]nineteen into which the Greek is divided.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Within recent years all has been done that can possibly be done to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]secure perfect copies of both Greek and Latin. In 1859 the Royal
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Academy of Berlin commissioned a scholar named Mordtmann to secure a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_papier maché_ cast of the Latin, and to transcribe the Greek. He
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]failed in both attempts, and declared that the casts would ruin the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]original.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Napoleon III. commissioned George Perrot and Edmund Guillaume to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]explore Asia Minor. In their work above mentioned they give a facsimile
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]copy of the whole of the Latin, and of as much of the Greek as they
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]could get at. Their plates were the basis of an edition of the text by
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Mommsen in 1865, and another by Bergk in 1873, and of the text given in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the _Corpus Inscriptionum Latinarum_.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]But Mommsen and the Berlin Academy were not satisfied. Carl Humann had
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]distinguished himself by his researches at Pergamos, and to him they
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]committed the task of securing casts of the whole of both texts. The
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]story of his achievement is extremely interesting. Difficulty after
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]difficulty was met and surmounted. And finally he succeeded in his
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]plan. With materials dug near-by he made plaster casts. The owners of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the Turkish houses he succeeded in inducing to allow their walls to be
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]so far torn away as to permit him to get at the entire Greek text. And
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]finally twenty great cases containing the whole series of casts were
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]sent away on pack mules to the coast and thence to Berlin. The Royal
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Academy now counts these casts among its chief treasures. This was
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in 1882. In 1883 Mommsen published his great critical edition of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]text, on which this edition is based. His work is almost final on the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]subject, but especially in the matter of conjectural fillings of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_lacunæ_ is subject to revision. But an inspection of the text
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]as given in this volume will show that we have the words of Augustus
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]almost in their entirety.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]At Apollonia, on the borders of Phrygia and Pisidia, has been found
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]another ruined temple, with remnants of the Greek version of this
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]inscription. At Apollonia the inscription originally covered seven
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]pages. Of these there are still legible the upper portions of pages
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]two, three, four and five. The correspondence between the text at
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Ancyra and that at Apollonia is almost exact, and where there is a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]divergence, it has been indicated.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]II. CHARACTER AND PURPOSE OF THE INSCRIPTION.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]German scholars have waged a fierce warfare over the question of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]literary character of the _Res Gestæ_, as Mommsen commonly calls
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]it. He himself refrains from assigning it decidedly to any class of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]composition. Is it epitaph, or a “statement of account,” or “political
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]statement”? Otto Hirschfeld contends strongly it is not an epitaph
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]because it contains no dates of birth or death, and is in the first
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]person. Wölfflin calls it a statement of account. Geppert sides with
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Hirschfeld. Bormann, Schmidt and Nissen all hold it to be an epitaph.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]And this appears to be the final agreement. The latest word is the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]discussion by Bormann, in 1895, in which he still maintains the epitaph
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]view. For these discussions, cf. the bibliography at the end of this
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]volume.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Of course it is an epitaph of unique character. It has certain striking
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]peculiarities, and specially of omission. There is no mention of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]domestic affairs. The wife of the Emperor is unnamed. Although in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]enumerating his honors and offices it was necessary to date events by
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the names of consuls, yet aside from this he mentions no person outside
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the imperial household, not even such favorites as Mæcenas and Agrippa.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]His foes, Brutus, Cassius and Antony, are several times alluded to,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]but never named. The same is true of Lepidus and Sextus Pompeius.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Unfortunate events are not noticed. His omission of the disaster to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the Roman arms under Varus has been severely criticised as an attempt
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to deceive; but if the inscription is really an epitaph one cannot
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]wonder at such silence. The omission of the dates of birth and death
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]has been variously explained. Some have thought that he meant his heirs
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to fill in any such gaps after his death, and to recast the whole into
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the third person. Or, it has been suggested that it was the desire of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Augustus to be counted a divinity, and that therefore he wished to pose
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]as one “without beginning of years, or end of days.” It certainly would
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]be incongruous to record the death of a god. With regard to his general
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]purpose Mommsen says: “No one would look for the arcana of empire in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]such a document, but for such things as an _imperator_ of mind
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]shrewd rather than lofty, and who skillfully bore the character of a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]great man while he himself was not great, wished the whole people, and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]especially the rabble, to believe about him.” Two purposes are manifest
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]throughout the document. One is to pose as a saviour of the state from
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]its foes, and not at all as a seeker after personal aggrandizement;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]another is to represent his whole authority as having been exercised
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]under constitutional forms. These two ideas appear again and again.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]III. DIVISIONS OF THE TEXT.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The text may be roughly divided into three sections. Chapters one
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to fourteen give the various offices held by Augustus, and the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]honors bestowed upon him; chapters fifteen to twenty-four recount
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]his expenditures for the good of the state and the people; and the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]remaining chapters, twenty-five to thirty-five, give the statement
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of his various achievements in war, and his works of a more peaceful
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]character. This classification will not hold rigorously, but is true in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the main.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The division into chapters or paragraphs is marked in the Latin text
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]by making the first line of each chapter project a little to the left
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the remaining lines. Each such paragraph is relatively complete.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]And the use of such a topical method marks a new manner of composition
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]quite different from the old annalistic style of Roman historiography.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]IV. THE GREEK VERSION.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]George Kaibel has made a special study of the Greek version, and is led
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to the opinion that it was made by a Roman rather than by a Greek. It
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]is a grammar and dictionary rendering, rather than the idiomatic work
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of one quite at home in the use of Greek. This conclusion is based
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]upon linguistic grounds. A further question remains as to where this
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]translation was made, whether at Rome or in the provinces. The fact of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the identity of the two copies at Apollonia and at Ancyra would seem to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]indicate a common Roman source.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]V. THE SUPPLEMENT.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]This is poorly written both in the Latin and in the Greek; and it is
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]also a very imperfect summary of the document, summing up only what
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]was spent upon games, donations and buildings. The fact that it is in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the third person also proves that it is not the work of Augustus. The
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]reckoning by denarii rather than by sesterces points to a Greek origin,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and the mention of favors shown by Augustus to provincial towns (cf. c.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]4 and notes) would indicate one outside of Rome.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]VI. TRUSTWORTHINESS OF THE INSCRIPTION.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The corroborations of the inscription by other inscriptions, coins and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]later historians, as well as by allusions in contemporary literature,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]form an interesting study. And the trustworthiness of the record
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]becomes more manifest the more one compares its statements with those
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of other writers. Only one point has been found where Augustus makes
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]what might be challenged as a perversion of fact. (Cf. c. 2, note 16.)
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]VII. MASONS’ BLUNDERS.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]A number of apparent errors in the text are to be attributed in all
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]probability to the stone-cutters at Ancyra. Such are the superfluous
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_et_ of Latin ii, 2; _aede_ for _aedem_, iv, 22;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_quinquens_ for _quinquiens_, iv, 31; _ducenti_ for
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_ducentos_, iv, 45; _provicias_ for _provincias_, v, 11;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Tigrane_ for _Tigranem_, v, 31. εὔξησα for ἠύξησα, Gr. iv,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]8; Ῥωμάοις for Ῥωμαίοις, vii, 6; ὑπατον for ὑπάτων, vii, 15; ἄνδρας
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]μυριάδων for ἀνδρῶν μυριάδας, viii, 8; omission of τρὶς before χειλίας,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]ix, 13; ἐπεσκευσα for ἐπεσκευάσα, x, 18; omission of ναὸν before
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]ἀγοράν, xi, 10; επεύξησα for ἐπηύξησα, xiv, 4; omission of Ἀρτάξου, xv,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]3; μείσζονος for μείζονος, xv, 15; προκατηλειμένας for κατειλημένας,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]xv, 17; ἐπειταδε for ἐπίταδε, xvi, 11; βασιλεες for βασιλεῖς, xvi, 22;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]βασιλεις for βασιλεὺς, xvii, 4; ἐπείκειαν for ἐπιείκειαν, xviii, 5;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]ἀγορᾷ Σεβαστῇ for ἀγορὰ Σεβαστή, xix, 1.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]VIII. SIGNS AND ABBREVIATIONS.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The Latin and Greek texts are printed in such a way as to give the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]best idea practicable of their actual condition. Roman numerals denote
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the pages of the inscription, and the Arabic figures the lines. These
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]numerals and the chapter headings are no part of the inscription. The
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]projection of the first line of each chapter in the Latin is the only
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]method of marking the divisions in the original.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Parts of the Greek and Latin text included within brackets, [], are
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]conjectural restorations of the portions of the inscription which have
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]perished. The Greek generally is a guide to the Latin and _vice
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]versa_, for the instances are rare where both versions have been
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]lost. The textual notes show that not all scholars have reckoned the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]same number of missing letters. These variations are quite allowable,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]for it is impossible to say that just so many letters are missing in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]any given case, owing to the various sizes of different letters, and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]varying degrees of closeness of writing.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Where dots (...) occur, it signifies that Mommsen reckons as many
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]letters unrestored as there are dots.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The sign § indicates a mark in the original resembling a figure 7, or a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]very open 3.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The same sign in brackets [§] indicates an unfilled interval in the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]stone.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The apices over vowels in the Latin indicate similar marks in the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]original in the case of a, e, o and u, and in the case of i a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]prolongation of that letter above the line.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Where certain letters of the Latin text are italicized it indicates
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]that while they do not appear in the plaster casts, yet they were
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]traced by Alfred Domaszewski (a fellow-worker with Humann) on the stone
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]itself, by means of certain discolorations from paint, or gilding, or
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]weather, which marked the bottom of the incisions of the letters in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]several cases where the surface of the stone had been worn away.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]In the textual notes, B. stands for Bormann, G. for Geppert, S. for J.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Schmidt, Sk. for Seeck, W. for Wölfflin, Apoll. for the inscription at
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Apollonia, and Anc. for that at Ancyra.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The abbreviations of the names of authors and their works in the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]historical notes are indicated in the bibliography at the close of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]book.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]      II, 16. iis, S. quibus.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]      II, 17. me ludos aliquotiens, W. mihi ludos interdum; aliquotiens,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]              B. votivos modo.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]      II, 18. aliquotiens, W. interdum; aliquotiens consules, B. modo
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]              consules ejus anni.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]      II, 19. sacrificaverunt, B. sacrificia; W. supplicaverunt; semper,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]              B. concorditer; W. unanimiter.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]      II, 20. B. adds fecerunt.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]      II, 22. sacrosanctus ut essem ........ W. sacrosancta ut esset
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]              persona mea, or sacrosancta potestate ut essem.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]      II, 25. habuit, B. habuerat; cepi id, B. quod.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]      II, 26. qui civilis motus, B, suscepi qui id tumultus.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]      II, 27. ad comitia mea ......... B. propter mea comitia, or
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]              comitiorum caussa; Sk. inserts coeunte before ad.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]      II, 28. fertur, Sk. memoriae proditur; omits coeunte.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]      II, 29. reduci, B. reducis.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]      II, 32. B. inserts eo before die.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]      II, 33. redi, B. redieram.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]      II, 36. S. inserts ante after honos.

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]               tributus, Sk. titulos.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]     VIII, 17. οὗτος, W. σύνπας; ἀριθμὸς, S. ἀριθμῷ or ἀριθμὸν.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]        X, 22. S. inserts τοῦ before Κρόνου.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]        X, 23. S. inserts μου after πατρὸς.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]        X, 24. καυθεῖσαν ἐπὶ, S. καταφλεχθεῖσαν ἐν.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]      XII,  1. ἐψηφίσαντο, S. καὶ ἐψήφιστο.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]     XIII, 22. οἳ ἢ πρότερον ἢ, S. ὑπατικοὶ καὶ οἳ.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Below is a copy of the deeds of the divine Augustus, by which he
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]subjected the whole world to the dominion of the Roman people, and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the amounts which he expended upon the commonwealth and the Roman
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]people, as engraved upon two brazen columns which are set up at Rome.[1]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 1.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]In my twentieth year,[2] acting upon my own judgment[3] and at my
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]own expense,[4] I raised an army[5] by means of which I restored to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]liberty the commonwealth which had been oppressed by the tyranny of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]a faction.[6] On account of this the senate by laudatory decrees
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]admitted me to its order,[7] in the consulship of Gaius Pansa and Aulus
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Hirtius, and at the same time gave me consular rank in the expression
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of opinion,[8] and gave me the _imperium_.[9] It also voted that
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]I as propraetor,[10] together with the consuls, should see to it that
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the commonwealth suffered no harm.[11] In the same year, moreover, when
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]both consuls had perished in war, the people made me consul,[12] and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]triumvir for organizing the commonwealth.[13]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 2.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Those who killed my father[14] I drove into exile by lawful
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]judgments,[15] avenging their crime, and afterwards, when they waged
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]war against the commonwealth, I twice defeated them in battle.[16]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 3.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]I undertook civil and foreign wars by land and sea throughout the whole
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]world, and as victor I showed mercy to all surviving citizens.[17]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Foreign peoples, who could be pardoned with safety, I preferred to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]preserve rather than to destroy. About five hundred thousand Roman
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]citizens took the military oath of allegiance to me.[18] Of these I
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]have settled in colonies or sent back to their _municipia_,[19]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]upon the expiration of their terms of service,[20] somewhat over three
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]hundred thousand, and to all these I have given lands purchased by
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]me, or money for farms,[21] out of my own means. I have captured six
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]hundred ships, besides those which were smaller than triremes.[22]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 4.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Twice I have triumphed in the ovation,[23] and three times in the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]curule triumph,[24] and I have been twenty-one times saluted as
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]imperator.[25]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]After that, when the senate decreed me many triumphs,[26] I declined
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]them. Likewise I often deposited the laurels in the Capitol[27] in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]fulfilment of vows which I had also made in battle. On account of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]enterprises brought to a successful issue on land and sea by me, or
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]by my lieutenants under my auspices, the senate fifty-five times
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]decreed that there should be a thanksgiving to the immortal gods.[28]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The number of days, moreover, on which thanksgiving was rendered
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in accordance with the decree of the senate was eight hundred and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]ninety.[29] In my triumphs there have been led before my chariot nine
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]kings, or children of kings.[30] When I wrote these words I had been
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]thirteen times consul, and was in the thirty-seventh year of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]tribunitial power.[31]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 5.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The dictatorship which was offered to me by the people and the senate,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]both when I was absent and when I was present, in the consulship of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Marcus Marcellus and Lucius Arruntius, I did not accept.[32] At a time
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the greatest dearth of grain I did not refuse the charge of the food
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]supply, which I so administered that in a few days, at my own expense,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]I freed the whole people from the anxiety and danger in which they then
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]were.[33] The annual and perpetual consulship offered to me at that
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]time I did not accept.[34]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 6.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]During the consulship of Marcus Vinucius and Quintus Lucretius, and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]afterwards in that of Publius and Cnaeus Lentulus, and a third time in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]that of Paullus Fabius Maximus and Quintus Tubero, by the consent of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the senate and the Roman people I was voted the sole charge of the laws
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and of morals, with the fullest power;[35] but I accepted the proffer
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of no office which was contrary to the customs of the country.[36] The
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]measures of which the senate at that time wished me to take charge, I
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]accomplished in virtue of my possession of the tribunitial power.[37]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]In this office I five times associated with myself a colleague, with
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the consent of the senate.[38]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 7.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]For ten years in succession I was one of the triumvirs for organizing
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the commonwealth.[39] Up to that day on which I write these words
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]I have been _princeps_ of the senate through forty years.[40]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]I have been _pontifex maximus_,[41] augur,[42] a member of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]quindecemviral college of the sacred rites,[43] of the septemviral
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]college of the banquets,[44] an Arval Brother,[45] a member of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Titian sodality,[46] and a fetial.[47]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 8.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]In my fifth consulship, by order of the people and the senate, I
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]increased the number of the patricians.[48] Three times I have revised
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the list of the senate.[49] In my sixth consulship, with Marcus Agrippa
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]as colleague, I made a census of the people. I performed the lustration
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]after forty-one years. In this lustration the number of Roman citizens
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]was four million and sixty-three thousand.[50] Again assuming the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]consular power in the consulship of Gaius Censorinus and Gaius Asinius,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]I alone performed the lustration. At this census the number of Roman
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]citizens was four million, two hundred and thirty thousand.[51] A third
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]time, assuming the consular power in the consulship of Sextus Pompeius
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and Sextus Appuleius, with Tiberius Cæsar as colleague, I performed the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]lustration. At this lustration the number of Roman citizens was four
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]million, nine hundred and thirty-seven thousand.[52] By new legislation
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]I have restored many customs of our ancestors which had now begun to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]fall into disuse, and I have myself also committed to posterity many
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]examples worthy of imitation.[53]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 9.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The senate decreed that every fifth year vows for my good health should
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]be performed by the consuls and the priests. In accordance with these
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]vows games have been often celebrated during my lifetime, sometimes
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]by the four chief colleges, sometimes by the consuls.[54] In private,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]also, and as municipalities, the whole body of citizens have constantly
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]sacrificed at every shrine for my good health.[55]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 10.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]By a decree of the senate my name has been included in the Salian
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]hymn,[56] and it has been enacted by law that I should be sacrosanct,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and that as long as I live I should be invested with the tribunitial
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]power.[57] I refused to be made _pontifex maximus_ in the place of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]a colleague still living, when the people tendered me that priesthood
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]which my father held. I accepted that office after several years, when
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]he was dead who had seized it during a time of civil disturbance;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and at the comitia for my election, during the consulship of Publius
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Sulpicius and Gaius Valgius, so great a multitude assembled as, it is
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]said, had never before been in Rome.[58]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 11.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Close to the temples of Honor and Virtue, near the Capena gate, the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]senate consecrated in honor of my return an altar to Fortune the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Restorer, and upon this altar it ordered that the _pontifices_ and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the Vestal virgins should offer sacrifice yearly on the anniversary of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the day on which I returned into the city from Syria, in the consulship
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of Quintus Lucretius and Marcus Vinucius, and it called the day the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Augustalia, from our cognomen.[59]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 12.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]By a decree of the senate at the same time a part of the prætors and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]tribunes of the people with the consul Quintus Lucretius and leading
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]citizens were sent into Campania to meet me, an honor which up to this
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]time has been decreed to no one but me.[60] When I returned from Spain
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and Gaul after successfully arranging the affairs of those provinces,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in the consulship of Tiberius Nero and Publius Quintilius, the senate
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]voted that in honor of my return an altar of the Augustan Peace should
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]be consecrated in the Campus Martius, and upon this altar it ordered
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the magistrates and priests and vestal virgins to offer sacrifices on
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]each anniversary.[61]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 13.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Janus Quirinus, which it was the purpose of our fathers to close when
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]there was peace won by victory[62] throughout the whole empire of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the Roman people on land and sea, and which, before I was born, from
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the foundation of the city, was reported to have been closed twice
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in all,[63] the senate three times ordered to be closed while I was
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_princeps_.[64]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 14.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]My sons, the Cæsars Gaius and Lucius, whom fortune snatched from me in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]their youth,[65] the senate and Roman people, in order to do me honor,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]designated as consuls in the fifteenth year of each, with the intention
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]that they should enter upon that magistracy after five years.[66] And
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the senate decreed that from the day in which they were introduced into
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the forum they should share in the public counsels.[67] Moreover the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]whole body of the Roman knights gave them the title, _principes_
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the youth, and gave to each a silver buckler and spear.[68]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 15.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]To each man of the Roman _plebs_ I paid three hundred sesterces
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in accordance with the last will of my father;[69] and in my own name,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]when consul for the fifth time, I gave four hundred sesterces from
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the spoils of the wars;[70] again, moreover, in my tenth consulship I
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]gave from my own estate four hundred sesterces to each man by way of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_congiarium_;[71] and in my eleventh consulship I twelve times
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]made distributions of food, buying grain at my own expense;[72] and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in the twelfth year of my tribunitial power I three times gave four
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]hundred sesterces to each man.[73] These my donations have never
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]been made to less than two hundred and fifty thousand men.[74] In my
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]twelfth consulship and the eighteenth year of my tribunitial power I
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]gave to three hundred and twenty thousand of the city _plebs_
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]sixty _denarii_ apiece.[75] In the colonies of my soldiers, when
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]consul for the fifth time, I gave to each man a thousand sesterces from
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the spoils; about a hundred and twenty thousand men in the colonies
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]received that triumphal donation.[76] When consul for the thirteenth
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]time I gave sixty _denarii_ to the _plebs_ who were at that
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]time receiving public grain; these men were a little more than two
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]hundred thousand in number.[77][78]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 16.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]For the lands which in my fourth consulship, and afterwards in the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]consulship of Marcus Crassus and Cnæus Lentulus, the augur, I assigned
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to soldiers, I paid money to the _municipia_. The sum which I paid
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]for Italian farms was about six hundred million sesterces, and that for
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]lands in the provinces was about two hundred and sixty millions.[79]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Of all those who have established colonies of soldiers in Italy or
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in the provinces I am the first and only one within the memory of my
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]age, to do this. And afterward in the consulship of Tiberius Nero and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Cnæus Piso, and also in that of Gaius Antistius and Decimus Lælius,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and in that of Gaius Calvisius and Lucius Pasienus, and in that of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Lucius Lentulus and Marcus Messala, and in that of Lucius Caninius and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Quintus Fabricius, I gave gratuities in money to the soldiers whom I
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]sent back to their _municipia_ at the expiration of their terms
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of service, and for this purpose I freely spent four hundred million
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]sesterces.[80]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 17.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Four times I have aided the public treasury from my own means, to such
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]extent that I have furnished to those in charge of the treasury one
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]hundred and fifty million sesterces.[81] And in the consulship of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Marcus Lepidus and Lucius Arruntius I paid into the military treasury
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]which was established by my advice that from it gratuities might be
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]given to soldiers who had served a term of twenty or more years, one
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]hundred and seventy million sesterces from my own estate.[82]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 18.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Beginning with that year in which Cnæus and Publius Lentulus were
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]consuls, when the imposts failed, I furnished aid sometimes to a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]hundred thousand men, and sometimes to more, by supplying grain or
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]money for the tribute from my own land and property.[83]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 19.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]I constructed[84] the Curia,[85] and the Chalcidicum adjacent
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]thereto,[86] the temple of Apollo on the Palatine, with its
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]porticoes,[87] the temple of the divine Julius,[88] the Lupercal,[89]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the portico to the Circus of Flaminius, which I allowed to bear the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]name, Portico Octavia, from his name who constructed the earlier one
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in the same place;[90] the Pulvinar at the Circus Maximus,[91] the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]temples of Jupiter the Vanquisher[92] and Jupiter the Thunderer, on the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Capitol,[93] the temple of Quirinus,[94] the temples of Minerva and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Juno Regina and of Jupiter Libertas, on the Aventine,[95] the temple of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the Lares on the highest point of the Via Sacra,[96] the temple of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]divine Penates on the Velian hill,[97] the temple of Youth,[98] and the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]temple of the Great Mother on the Palatine.[99]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 20.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The Capitol and the Pompeian theatre have been restored by me at
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]enormous expense for each work, without any inscription of my name.[100]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Aqueducts which were crumbling in many places by reason of age I have
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]restored, and I have doubled the water which bears the name Marcian
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]by turning a new spring into its course.[101] The Forum Julium and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the basilica which was between the temple of Castor and the temple
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of Saturn, works begun and almost completed by my father, I have
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]finished; and when that same basilica was consumed by fire, I began
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]its reconstruction on an enlarged site, inscribing it with the names
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of my sons; and if I do not live to complete it, I have given orders
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]that it be completed by my heirs.[102] In accordance with a decree of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the senate, while consul for the sixth time, I have restored eighty-two
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]temples of the gods, passing over none which was at that time in need
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of repair.[103] In my seventh consulship I constructed the Flaminian
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]way from the city to Ariminum, and all the bridges except the Mulvian
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and Minucian.[104]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 21.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Upon private ground I have built with the spoils of war the temple
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of Mars the Avenger, and the Augustan Forum.[105] Beside the temple
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of Apollo, I built upon ground, bought for the most part at my own
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]expense, a theatre, to bear the name of Marcellus, my son-in-law.[106]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]From the spoils of war I have consecrated gifts in the Capitol, and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in the temple of the divine Julius, and in the temple of Apollo, and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in the temple of Vesta, and in the temple of Mars the Avenger; these
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]gifts have cost me about a hundred million sesterces.[107] In my fifth
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]consulship I remitted to the _municipia_ and Italian colonies the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]thirty-five thousand pounds given me as coronary gold on the occasion
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of my triumphs, and thereafter, as often as I was proclaimed imperator,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]I did not accept the coronary gold which the _municipia_ and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]colonies voted to me as kindly as before.[108]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 22.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Three times in my own name, and five times in that of my sons or
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]grandsons, I have given gladiatorial exhibitions; in these exhibitions
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]about ten thousand men have fought.[109] Twice in my own name,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and three times in that of my grandson, I have offered the people
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the spectacle of athletes gathered from all quarters.[110] I have
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]celebrated games four times in my own name, and twenty-three times
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in the turns of other magistrates.[111] In behalf of the college of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]quindecemvirs, I, as master of the college, with my colleague Agrippa,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]celebrated the Secular Games in the consulship of Gaius Furnius and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Gaius Silanus.[112] When consul for the thirteenth time, I first
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]celebrated the Martial games, which since that time the consuls have
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]given in successive years.[113] Twenty-six times in my own name,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]or in that of my sons and grandsons, I have given hunts of African
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]wild beasts in the circus, the forum, the amphitheatres, and about
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]thirty-five hundred beasts have been killed.[114]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 23.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]I gave the people the spectacle of a naval battle beyond the Tiber,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]where now is the grove of the Cæsars.[115] For this purpose an
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]excavation was made eighteen hundred feet long and twelve hundred
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]wide. In this contest thirty beaked ships, triremes or biremes, were
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]engaged, besides more of smaller size. About three thousand men fought
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in these vessels in addition to the rowers.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 24.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]In the temples of all the cities of the province of Asia, I, as victor,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]replaced the ornaments of which he with whom I was at war had taken
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]private possession when he despoiled the temples.[116] Silver statues
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of me, on foot, on horseback and in quadrigas, which stood in the city
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to the number of about eighty, I removed, and out of their money value,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]I placed golden gifts in the temple of Apollo in my own name, and in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the names of those who had offered me the honor of the statues.[117]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 25.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]I have freed the sea from pirates. In that war with the slaves I
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]delivered to their masters for punishment about thirty thousand
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]slaves who had fled from their masters and taken up arms against the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]state.[118] The whole of Italy voluntarily took the oath of allegiance
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to me, and demanded me as leader in that war in which I conquered at
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Actium. The provinces of Gaul, Spain, Africa, Sicily and Sardinia swore
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the same allegiance to me.[119] There were more than seven hundred
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]senators who at that time fought under my standards, and among these,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]up to the day on which these words are written, eighty-three have
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]either before or since been made consuls, and about one hundred and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]seventy have been made priests.[120]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 26.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]I have extended the boundaries of all the provinces of the Roman people
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]which were bordered by nations not yet subjected to our sway.[121] I
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]have reduced to a state of peace the Gallic and Spanish provinces, and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Germany, the lands enclosed by the ocean from Gades to the mouth of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the Elbe.[122] The Alps from the region nearest the Adriatic as far as
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the Tuscan Sea I have brought into a state of peace, without waging an
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]unjust war upon any people.[123] My fleet has navigated the ocean from
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the mouth of the Rhine as far as the boundaries of the Cimbri, where
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]before that time no Roman had ever penetrated by land or sea;[124] and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the Cimbri and Charydes and Semnones and other German peoples of that
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]section, by means of legates, sought my friendship and that of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Roman people.[125] By my command and under my auspices two armies at
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]almost the same time have been led into Ethiopia and into Arabia, which
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]is called “the Happy,” and very many of the enemy of both peoples have
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]fallen in battle, and many towns have been captured. Into Ethiopia the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]advance was as far as Nabata, which is next to Meroe.[126] In Arabia
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the army penetrated as far as the confines of the Sabaei, to the town
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Mariba.[127]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 27.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]I have added Egypt to the empire of the Roman people.[128] Of greater
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Armenia, when its king Artaxes was killed I could have made a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]province, but I preferred, after the example of our fathers, to deliver
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]that kingdom to Tigranes, the son of king Artavasdes, and grandson of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]king Tigranes; and this I did through Tiberius Nero, who was then my
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]son-in-law.[129] And afterwards, when the same people became turbulent
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and rebellious, they were subdued by Gaius, my son, and I gave the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]sovereignty over them to king Ariobarzanes, the son of Artabazes, king
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the Medes, and after his death to his son Artavasdes. When he was
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]killed I sent into that kingdom Tigranes, who was sprung from the royal
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]house of the Armenians.[130] I recovered all the provinces across the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Adriatic Sea, which extend toward the east, and Cyrenaica, at that time
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]for the most part in the possession of kings, together with Sicily and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Sardinia, which had been engaged in a servile war.[131]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 28.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]I have established colonies of soldiers[132] in Africa, Sicily,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Macedonia, the two Spains, Achaia, Asia, Syria, Gallia Narbonensis and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Pisidia.[133] Italy also has twenty-eight colonies established under
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]my auspices, which within my lifetime have become very famous and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]populous.[134]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 29.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]I have recovered from Spain and Gaul, and from the Dalmatians, after
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]conquering the enemy, many military standards which had been lost by
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]other leaders.[135] I have compelled the Parthians to give up to me
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the spoils and standards of three Roman armies, and as suppliants to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]seek the friendship of the Roman people. Those standards, moreover,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]I have deposited in the sanctuary which is in the temple of Mars the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Avenger.[136]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 30.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The Pannonian peoples, whom before I became _princeps_, no army
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the Roman people had ever attacked, were defeated by Tiberius Nero,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]at that time my son-in-law and legate; and I brought them under
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]subjection to the empire of the Roman people,[137] and extended the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]boundaries of Illyricum to the bank of the river Danube.[138] When an
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]army of the Dacians crossed this river, it was defeated and destroyed,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and afterwards my army, led across the Danube, compelled the Dacian
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]people to submit to the sway of the Roman people.[139]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 31.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Embassies have been many times sent to me from the kings of India, a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]thing never before seen in the case of any ruler of the Romans.[140]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Our friendship has been sought by means of ambassadors by the Bastarnae
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and the Scythians, and by the kings of the Sarmatae, who are on either
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]side of the Tanais, and by the kings of the Albani, the Hiberi, and the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Medes.[141]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 32.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]To me have betaken themselves as suppliants the kings of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Parthians, Tiridates, and later, Phraates, the son of king
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Phraates;[142] of the Medes, Artavasdes;[143] of the Adiabeni,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Artaxares;[144] of the Britons, Dumnobellaunus and Tim_____;[145]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the Sicambri, Maelo;[146] and of the Marcomanian Suevi,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]__________rus.[147] Phraates, king of the Parthians, son of Orodes,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]sent all his children and grandchildren into Italy to me, not because
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]he had been conquered in war, but rather seeking our friendship
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]by means of his children as pledges.[148] Since I have been
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_princeps_ very many other races have made proof of the good
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]faith of the Roman people, who never before had had any interchange of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]embassies and friendship with the Roman people.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 33.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]From me the peoples of the Parthians and of the Medes have received
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the kings they asked for through ambassadors, the chief men of those
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]peoples: the Parthians, Vonones, the son of king Phraates, and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]grandson of king Orodes;[149] the Medes, Ariobarzanes, the son of king
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Artavasdes, and grandson of king Ariobarzanes.[150]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 34.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]In my sixth and seventh consulships, when I had put an end to the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]civil wars, after having obtained complete control of affairs by
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]universal consent, I transferred the commonwealth from my own dominion
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to the authority of the senate and Roman people.[151] In return for
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]this favor on my part I received by decree of the senate the title
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Augustus,[152] the door-posts of my house were publicly decked with
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]laurels, a civic crown was fixed above my door,[153] and in the Julian
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Curia was placed a golden shield, which, by its inscription, bore
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]witness that it was given to me by the senate and Roman people on
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]account of my valor, clemency, justice and piety.[154] After that time
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]I excelled all others in dignity, but of power I held no more than
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]those also held who were my colleagues in any magistracy.[155]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 35.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]While I was consul for the thirteenth time the senate and the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]equestrian order and the entire Roman people gave me the title of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]father of the fatherland, and decreed that it should be inscribed upon
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the vestibule of my house and in the Curia, and in the Augustan Forum
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]beneath the quadriga which had been, by decree of the senate, set up
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in my honor.[156] When I wrote these words I was in my seventy-sixth
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]year.[157]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]SUPPLEMENT.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 1.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The sum of the money which he gave in to the treasury or to the Roman
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]people, or to discharged soldiers, was six hundred million denarii.[158]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 2.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]He constructed new works as follows: the temples of Mars, of Jupiter
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the Thunderer and the Vanquisher, of Apollo, of the divine Julius,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of Quirinus, of Minerva, of Juno Regina, of Jupiter Libertas, of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Lares, of the divine Penates, of Youth, and of the Mother of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]gods, the Lupercal, the Pulvinar in the Circus, the Curia with the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Chalcidicum, the Augustan Forum, the Basilica Julia, the Theatre of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Marcellus, the Portico on the Palatine, the Portico in the Flaminian
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Circus, the grove of the Cæsars beyond the Tiber.[159]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 3.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]He restored the Capitol, and sacred structures to the number of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]eighty-two, the Theatre of Pompey, the aqueducts, the Flaminian
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Way.[160]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c. 4.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]His expenses for theatrical representations, for gladiatorial and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]athletic exhibitions, for chases and the naval combat,[161] also for
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]gifts in money to the colonies and cities of Italy,[162] to provincial
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]cities suffering from earthquake or conflagrations,[163] and to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]individual friends and to senators, whose property he raised to the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]standard,[164] were innumerable.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]CHRONOLOGICAL TABLE.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ](_Roman numerals refer to chapters._)
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  A. U. C.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  706. Made _pontifex_, VI.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  710. Raises army at his own cost, I; gives to each citizen 300
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  sesterces, according to will of Julius Cæsar, XV.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  711. Enters senate, receives consular rank, and the _imperium_,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  becomes _propraetor_, _imperator_, consul, I; triumvir, I
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  and VII; exiles murderers of Julius Cæsar, II.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  712. War of Philippi, II; builds the curia, XIX, app. II.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  714. _Imperator_ second and third times; ovation, IV.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  716. Recovers Sardinia, XXVII.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  718. The Sicilian war, III and XIX; fourth time _imperator_, IV;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  punishes revolted slaves, XXV; recovers Sicily, XXVII; ovation, IV;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  receives tribunitial power, X, cf. VI; builds temple of Apollo on the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  Palatine, XIX, app. II.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  721. Fifth time _imperator_? IV; recovers standards from
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  Dalmatians, XXIX.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  722. Becomes leader against Antony, XXV.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  723. Victory of Actium; clemency as victor, III; sixth time
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  _imperator_, IV.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  724. Fourth consulship; veterans colonized, XVI; provinces east of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  the Adriatic, and Cyrenae recovered; Egypt annexed, XXVII; Artavasdes
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  the Mede and Tiridates the Parthian flee to Augustus, XXXII;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  ornaments replaced in temples of Asia, XXIV.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  725. Fifth consulship, VIII, XV, XXI; seventh time _imperator_;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  triple triumph, IV; declines coronary gold, XXI; gives to 120,000
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  colonized soldiers 1,000 sesterces apiece; gives the people 400
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  sesterces each, XV; gives gladiatorial show, XXII; consecrates gifts
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  in various temples, XXI; closes temple of Janus, XIII; name placed in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  Salian hymn, X; increases number of patricians, VIII.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  726. Sixth consulship, VIII, XX, XXXIV. Takes census; revises list
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  of senators, VIII; made _princeps senatus_, VII; restores city
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  temples, XX, app. III; gives money to the treasury, XVII; gives
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  gladiatorial and athletic shows, XXII; games vowed and celebrated for
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  health of Augustus, IX; restores the commonwealth to the senate and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  people, XXXIV.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  727. Seventh consulship, XX, XXXIV. Continuation of transfer of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  power to senate and people; is called Augustus; door-posts decked
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  with laurel; civic crown and golden shield accorded, XXXIV; repairs
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  Flaminian Way, XX, app. III; melts down silver statues for offerings,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  XXIV.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  729. Eighth time _imperator_; refuses triumph, IV; closes temple
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  of Janus the second time, XIII; Arabian expedition, XXVI.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  730. Tenth consulship; gives the people 400 sesterces each.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  731. Eleventh consulship; twelve times supplies food for citizens,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  XV, cf. V; Ethiopian expedition, XXVI.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  732. Consulship of Marcus Marcellus and Lucius Arruntius; refuses
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  annual and perpetual consulship; also the dictatorship; accepts
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  the administration of grain supply, V; dedicates temple of Jupiter
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  Tonans, XIX.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  733. Refuses consulship? V.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  734. Receives embassy from India, XXXI; ninth time _imperator_?
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  refuses a triumph, IV; recovers standards from Parthia, XXIX; gives
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  Armenia Major to Tigranes, XXVII.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  735. Quintus Lucretius and Marcus Vinucius consuls; altar of Fortuna
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  Redux consecrated; Augustalia established, XI; deputation of leading
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  men meet Augustus in Campania, XII; declines the custody of laws and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  morals, VI.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  736. Cnaeus and Publius Lentulus consuls, VI, XVIII; remits tribute,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  XVIII; again declines custody of laws and morals; associates Agrippa
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  in tribunitial power, VI.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  737. Gaius Furnius and Gaius Silanus consuls; secular games, XXII.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  738. Augustus supplies money to the treasury, XVII; gives
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  gladiatorial show, XXII; dedicates temple of Quirinus, XIX, app. II.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  739. Tenth time _imperator_, IV.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  740. Marcus Crassus and Cnaeus Lentulus consuls; pays provincials for
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  lands taken for veterans.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  741. Tiberius Nero and Publius Quintilius consuls, XII; deposits
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  laurel in the Capitol, IV; altar of the Augustan Peace dedicated,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  XII; again associates Agrippa in tribunitial power, VI.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  742. Gaius Sulpicius and Gaius Valgius consuls, X; twelfth year of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  tribunitial power, XV; eleventh time _imperator_, IV; made
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  _pontifex maximus_, X; gives gladiatorial show, XXII; gives the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  people 400 sesterces each, XV.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  743. Paullus Fabius Maximus and Quintus Tubero consuls, VI; twelfth
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  time _imperator_, IV; for the third time refuses the custody of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  laws and morals, VI; dedicates theater of Marcellus, XXI, app. II.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  745. Thirteenth time _imperator_; deposits the laurel in temple
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  of Jupiter Feretrius, IV; Tiberius Nero subdues the Pannonians, XXX.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  746. Gaius Censorinus and Gaius Asinius consuls; second census taken;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  list of senate revised, VIII; children of Phraates sent to Rome;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  Maelo, King of the Sicambri, surrenders himself, XXXII; fourteenth
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  time _imperator_; refuses a triumph, IV.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  747. Tiberius Nero and Cnaeus Piso consuls; veterans discharged, with
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  gratuities, XVI; Alpine peoples added to the empire, XXVI; gives
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  gladiatorial show, XXII.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  748. Gaius Antistius and Decimus Laelius consuls; veterans
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  discharged, with gratuities, XVI; associates Tiberius in tribunitial
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  power, VI.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  749. Eighteenth year of tribunitial power; twelfth consulship; gives
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  sixty denarii each to 320,000 citizens; Gaius Cæsar consul designate,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  made prince of the youth, received into senate, XIV; aqueducts
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  repaired, XX, app. III.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  750. Gaius Calvisius and Lucius Passienus consuls; veterans
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  discharged, with gratuities, XVI.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  751. Lucius Lentulus and Marcus Messala consuls; veterans discharged,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  with gratuities, XVI.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  752. Thirteenth consulship, XV, XXII, XXXV; Lucius Caninius and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  Quintus Fabricius consuls; veterans discharged, with gratuities,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  XVI; gives the citizens sixty denarii each, XV; Lucius Cæsar
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  consul designate, prince of the youth, and admitted to senate,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  XIV; dedicates temple of Mars Ultor, XXI, app. II; martial games
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  instituted, XXII; naval contest exhibited, XXIII; title _pater
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  patriae_ conferred, XXXV.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  755. Lucius Cæsar dies, XIV, cf. XX; fifteenth time _imperator_,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  IV; Armenia subdued by Gaius Cæsar and given to Ariobarzanes, XXVII.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  757. Gaius Cæsar dies, XIV, cf. XX; again associates Tiberius in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  tribunitial power, VI.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  758. Fleet penetrates to limits of the Cimbri; the Cimbri, Charudes
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  and Semnones send ambassadors, XXVI; King Vonones given to the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  Parthians, XXXIII.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  759. Marcus Lepidus and Lucius Arruntius consuls, XVII; seventeenth
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  time _imperator_, IV; Dacians subdued, XXX; gives gladiatorial
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  show, XXII; military treasury established, XVII.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  762. Nineteenth time _imperator_, IV.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  766. Associates Tiberius the third time in tribunitial power, VI.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  767. Sextus Pompeius and Sextus Appuleius consuls, VIII;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  thirty-seventh year of tribunitial power, IV; seventy-sixth year of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  Augustus, XXXV; third census taken; list of senate revised, VIII.

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]NOTES:
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][1] This title at Ancyra extends over the first three pages of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Latin, that is over so much of the inscription as is on the left wall
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the pronaos; the Greek title extends over seventeen of the nineteen
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]pages of the Greek version.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]In its present form, the title cannot be the same as that over the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]original at Rome. All from “as engraved” is certainly an addition,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]probably made by the Galatian legate who ordered the magistrates of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Ancyra to have the inscription placed on the temple of Augustus.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The last two words in the Latin (placed first in the English), were
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]probably inserted only by a blunder at Ancyra. “A copy subjoined,”
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]doubtless stood in the legate’s letter, just as we might write “see
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]enclosure.” But what of the remainder of the inscription, “Of the deeds
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]... Roman people”? It is hardly conceivable that this was the title
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the inscription at Rome, because it embraces only two of the three
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]parts into which the subject-matter falls. It covers the achievements
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and the expenditures of Augustus; in reverse order, however, from that
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the document itself; and it omits any allusion to the subject-matter
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the first fourteen chapters, which have to do with the offices and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]honors conferred upon Augustus.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]It is impossible to say what was the superscription at Rome. Possibly
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]there was none. The name of Augustus, most likely, was conspicuous
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]somewhere in connection with the front of the mausoleum, and this
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]inscription may very well have been devoid of title.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][2] Augustus was nineteen years old on Sept. 23, 710.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][3] Cicero (_Ad Att._ XVI, 8, 1,) on Nov. 1, 710, writes: “I have
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]letters from Octavian; great things are doing; he has led over to his
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]views the veterans of Casilinum and Calatia.” Cf. Vell. II, 61. Dio
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]XLVI, 29.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][4] Cf. Cic. (_Phil._ III, 2, 3), “The young Cæsar, without our
[ΕΚΤΟΣ](the senate’s) advice or consent, raised an army and poured forth his
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]patrimony.”
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][5] Gardthausen, _Aug._ 1er Th. 2er Bd. p. 524, thinks that this
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]beginning the Res Gestae with the raising of an army, is an admission
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the military foundation of the principate.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][6] Such a statement is part of Augustus’ scheme to pose as a restorer
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the old order. He makes Brutus, Cassius, Pompey and Antony public
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]enemies.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][7] Cicero says (_Phil._ V, 17, 46), that on Jan. 1, 711, “the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]senate voted that Gaius Cæsar, son of Gaius, pontiff, should be a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]senator, and hold praetorian rank in speaking.” Dio (XLVI, 29), says
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]that on Jan. 2 or 3, “Cæsar was made senator as a quaestor.”
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][8] Livy (_Ep._ CXVIII), “he received the consular ornaments.”
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]App. (_B. C._ III, 51) adds that he was given consular rank in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]speaking. Cf. Mommsen, _Röm. St._, I, pp. 442, 443.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][9] Cf. Cic. (_Phil._ ii, 8, 20), “The senate gave Gaius Cæsar the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]fasces.” Cf. Tac. _Ann._ I, 10; Livy, _Ep._ CXVIII.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][10] App. _B. C._ III, 51. Vell. II, 61.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][11] The formula by which in emergencies, extraordinary powers were
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]given to the ordinary magistrates. This measure had since 216 B. C.,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]entirely superseded the old custom of appointing a dictator. (Cf. note
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][32]) Chap. V. The present formula, however, had been employed long
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]before the disuse of the dictatorship. Cf. Livy III, 4; VI, 19. This
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]extraordinary commission was not restricted to the consuls. Cf. Cæs.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_B. C._ I, 5.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][12] Hirtius was killed April 16, 711, and Pansa died of wounds
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]received on the 15th, in the fighting against Antonius. Cæsar
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Octavianus and Q. Pedius were elected consuls Aug. 19, 711. Dio
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]LVI, 30; C. I. L. I, p. 400 = x, 8375; Tac. _Ann._ I, 9; Suet.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Aug._ 100. Vell. (II, 65), says the election was on Sept. 22. But
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Macrobius, (_Sat._ I, 35, 25), assigns the fact that he was made
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]consul in the month Sextilis, as one of the reasons why the name of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]that month was changed to August.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][13] C. I. L. I, p. 466 and App. _B. C._ IV, 7, fix the formal
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]ratification of the triumvirate by the people, as having been proposed
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]by the tribune Publius Titius and carried in a public assembly on Nov.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]27, 711.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][14] An instance of Augustus’ avoiding the names of his enemies; here,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]particularly, Brutus and Cassius.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][15] The _Lex Pedia_, Sept., 711, named from Augustus’ colleague
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in the consulship, constituted an extraordinary tribunal for this class
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of offenders: the penalty was interdiction from fire and water,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_i. e._, outlawry. Livy, _Ep._ CXX; Vell. II, 69; App. III, 95;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Suet. _Aug._ 10; Dio XLVI, 49.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][16] The only instance in the Res Gestae of a palpable distortion of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]fact. The battles at Philippi, in November, 712, are referred to. For
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the date see Gardthausen, _Aug._ 2er Th. 1er Halbband, p. 80. In
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the first fight, Suetonius says (_Aug._ 13), that Cæsar hardly
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]escaped, ill and naked, from his camp to the wing of Antony’s army.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]He was ill, and had to be carried in a litter, according to Plutarch,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Brut._ p. 41. In _Antony_, 22, Plutarch says: “In the first
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]battle, Cæsar was completely routed by Brutus, his camp taken, he
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]himself very narrowly escaping by flight.” The decisive defeat of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Republicans was twenty days later.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][17] The text here is conjectural. Mommsen is almost alone in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]holding to “surviving,” Zumpt, in his edition of 1869, had read
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]“suppliant” (_supplicibus_), Bergk, in 1873, “asking pardon”
[ΕΚΤΟΣ](_deprecantibus_). Hirschfeld, the same sense, (_veniam
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]petentibus_). Seeck insists on the latter reading, in spite of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Mommsen’s arguments for his own choice. Augustus did not spare all
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]surviving citizens either after Philippi or Actium, cf. Dio LI, 2:
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]After Actium “of the senators and knights, and other leading men, who
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in any way had helped Antony, he fined some, many he killed, some he
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]spared.” For his conduct after Philippi, cf. Suet. _Aug._ 13. But
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]a coin of 727 (Eckhel VI, 88, Cohen I, p. 66, No. 30), has CÆSAR COS
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]VII CIVIBUS SERVATEIS, “Cæsar for the seventh time consul, the citizens
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]having been preserved.” It commemorates the civic crown given to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Augustus, cf. c. XXXIV. There are other coins with OB CIVES SERVATOS,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]“On account of the preservation of the citizens.”
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][18] This fact is one of the few which the latest text, based on
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Humann’s work, alone establishes. Merivale’s comment on the relation
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of Augustus to the army is noteworthy: “Their hero (Julius Cæsar)
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]discarded the defence of the legions, and a few months witnessed his
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]assassination. Augustus learned circumspection from the failure of his
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]predecessor’s enterprise. He organized a military establishment of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]which he made himself the permanent head; to him every legionary swore
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]personal fidelity; every officer depended upon his direct appointment.”
[ΕΚΤΟΣ](C. XXXII.)
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][19] C. 15 states the number colonized at 120,000. The 200,000 over
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and above the 300,000 here named, are accounted for in the twenty-five
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]legions, 150,000 men in service at his death, leaving only 50,000 as
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the number who died in service or were dishonorably discharged during
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the long rule of Augustus. For a study of the strength and disposition
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the Roman army at the death of Augustus, cf. Mommsen’s R. G., pp.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]67-76.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][20] The term of service in 741, was twelve years for praetorian
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]soldiers and sixteen for legionaries, raised in 758 to sixteen and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]twenty years respectively. Cf. c. 17, N. 2.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][21] The reading of Wölfflin and others (see textual note) would give
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]instead of “lands purchased by me,” “I have assigned lands,” and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]instead of “money for farms, out of my own means” “money for reward
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of service.” Bormann, _Schr. Nachl._ p. 18-20, does not think
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]that Augustus meant to state that he paid these charges from private
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]sources, but believes that such a statement would be irrelevant in this
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]section, if true, and an anticipation of cc. 15 and 16.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][22] Sextus Pompeius lost thirty ships at Mylae, and at Naulochus, out
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of three hundred which he had, eighteen were sunk and the rest, with
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the exception of seventeen, burned or captured. Cf. App. _B. C._
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]V, 108, 118, 121. Plut. _Ant._ 68, says that Augustus took 300
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]ships at Actium. These captures give, in round numbers, 600 vessels.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][23] The ovation was the lesser triumph. The general entered the city
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]clad as an ordinary magistrate, and on foot, or as here, (see the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Greek), on horseback, decked with myrtle. Suet. _Aug._ 22, says,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]these ovations were after Philippi, and the Sicilian war; the former in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]714, the latter, Nov. 13, 718. Cf. Dio XLVIII, 31, XLIX, 15; C. I. L.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]1, p. 461.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][24] In the curule triumph, for important victories, the general was
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]vested in purple, and rode in a four-horse chariot, preceded by the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]fasces. These three triumphs were celebrated on the 13th, 14th and 15th
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of August, 725, for the Dalmatian successes, the victory of Actium and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the capture of Alexandria. Cf. C. I. L. I, p. 328 and 478. Prop. II,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]1, 31, ff, gives an eye-witness’ account of the second day. Cf. Livy,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Ep._ CXXXIII; Suet. _Aug._ 22; Verg. _Aen._ VIII; 714,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Dio LI, 21.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][25] The acclamation as _imperator_, on account of success in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]war, must be carefully distinguished from the title used as a prefix
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to the name and as a mark of perpetual authority. The title imperator
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]was regularly and permanently assumed at the beginning of each reign,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]after that of Augustus. To him it was formally assigned by the senate,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in Jan., 725. C. I. L., V, 1873: _Senatus populusque Romanus imp.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Cæsari, divi. Juli. f. cos. quinct. cos. design. sext. imp. sept.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]republica conservata._ The term thus had a double usage and meaning
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in such cases.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]It soon came about that only the _princeps_ could assume the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]special designation for military successes, no matter whether won by
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]him in person or not. Tacitus says, _Ann._ III, 74: “Tiberius
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]allowed Blaesus to be saluted as imperator by the legions. Augustus
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]conceded the title to some, but Tiberius’ allowing it to Blaesus was
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the last instance.” For a discussion of _Imperator_ as permanent
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]title, see Gardthausen, p. 527, and Merivale, _History of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Romans_, c. XXXI.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Most of the acclamations of Augustus as imperator can be traced. No
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Greek inscription records them. A list follows. In the later instances
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Tiberius was associated.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]I. April 15 (?) 711. After battles about Mutina. C. I. L. X, 8375 and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Dio XLVI, 38.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]II. Not traced.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]III. Before 717. Cohen, _Vipsan._ 3, gives a coin with the words
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_imp. divi Juli f. ter. III Vir v. p. c. M. Agrippa cos. desig._
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Agrippa entered his consulship Jan. 1, 717.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]IV. Probably connected with the Sicilian victory and ovation of 718.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]V. 720 or 721. Probably connected with Dalmatian victories of one of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]those years. Cf. C. I. L. V, 526.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]VI. From Sept. 2, 723, to 725. On account of Actium. Cf. Oros. VI, 19,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]14. C. I. L. X, 3826. _Imp. Cæsari divi f. imp. vi, cos. iii_
[ΕΚΤΟΣ](723). C. I. L. X, 4830, _imp. Cæsari divi f. cos. v_ (725)
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_imp. vi_.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]VII. From 725 to 729. C. I. L. VI, 873: _senatus populusque Romanus
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]imp. Cæsari divi Juli f. cos. quinct._ (725) _cos. desig. sex.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]imp. sept. republica conservata_. On account of Thracian and Dacian
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]victory of M. Licinius Crassus. Dio LI, 25, says: “Sacrifices and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]festivals were decreed to Cæsar and to Crassus. He did not, however, as
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]some say, take the name imperator. Cæsar alone assumed that.”
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]VIII. From 729 to 734. Two inscriptions at Nismes (Donat. 96, 6) read:
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_imp. Cæsari divi f. Augusto cos. nonum_ (729) _designato
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]decimum, imp. octavum_. Dio LIII, 26, says it was for a Celtic
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]victory of Marcus Vinicius.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]IX. From 734 to 739 (?) Coins have the inscription _Augustus Cæsar
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]div. f. Armen. capt. imp. viii_. These commemorate the Armenian
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]expedition of Tiberius in 734. Possibly Augustus took the title on
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]account of the return of the captured standards from Parthia, which he
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]accounted a greater triumph than many a victory in open warfare.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]X. 739 (?) to 742. C. I. L. V, 8088 and others: _Augustus imp. x,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]tribunicia potestate xi_. The latter falls in the years 742, 743.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Probably referable to successes in Rhætian war of 739.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]XI. 742. Coins (Cohen, n. 147-150) give: _imp. xi_. The causes
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]were the successes of Tiberius in Pannonia in 742. Dio LIV, 31.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]XII. 743 to 744. C. I. L., III, 3117: _imp. xii tr. pot. xiii_ and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]VI, 701, 702: _pontifex maximus, imp. xii cos. xi trib. pot. xiv_.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Referable to Germanic victory of Drusus. Dio LIV, 33.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]XIII. Tiberius Imp. 745. Suet., _Tib._ 9, says that Tiberius
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]received the oration for Pannonian and Dalmatian victories. Cf. Val. 5,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]5, 3. Dio LV, 2.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]XIV. Tiberius Imp. II. 746-755. Dio LV, 6, refers this acclamation
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to the Germanic victories of 746. Many coins, milestones and other
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]inscriptions of the period indicated mention this fourteenth
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]acclamation. Cf. C. I. L., II, 3827; 4931; V, 7243; 7817; VI, 1244.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]XV. 755. For the Armenian victory of C. Cæsar. Dio Cass. LV, 11. C. I.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]L. X, 3827; _pont. max., cos. iii (xiii) imp. xv, tr. p. xxv, p. p._
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]XVI. Untraced.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]XVII. Tiberius Imp. III. 759. Dio LV, 28, referring to the German
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]expedition of Tiberius in 759, says, “Nothing great was accomplished.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Yet both Augustus and Tiberius received the acclamation as imperators.”
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Cf. C. I. L. V. 6416.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]XVIII. Tiberius Imp. IV. Probably for successes in Illyricum.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]XIX. Tiberius Imp. V, 762. Dio LVI, 17, refers to the Dalmatian war. A
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]coin of 763-4 (Cohen n. 27) gives: _Ti. Cæsar August. f. imperat. v.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]pontifex, tribun. potestate xii_.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]XX. Tiberius Imp. VI. 765. The cause is not clear, probably for slight
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]successes of Tiberius and Germanicus against the Germans in 763, 764.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Dio LVI, 25. A Spanish milestone, C. I. L. II, 4868, gives the data.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]XXI. Tiberius Imp. VII. Tac. _Ann._ I, 9, says Augustus was
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]twenty-one times Imperator. A coin of Lyons (Cohen n. 35-38) has:
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Ti. Cæsar Augusti f. imperator VII_. This dates from the lifetime
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of Augustus. Tiberius did not receive a further acclamation.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][26] ᵃ After his own victory over the Cantabri, that of Varro over the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Salassi and that of M. Vinicius over the Germans, in 729. Cf. Florus,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]IV, 12, 53.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]ᵇ After the restoration of the standards by the Parthians in 734. Cf.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Borghesi II, 100 ff.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]ᶜ After the victories of Tiberius in Germany in 746. Dio LV, 6.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]ᵈ After the victories of Tiberius in Pannonia? Dio LVI, 17.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][27] A part of the ordinary ceremonial of the triumph. Cf. Mommsen,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Röm. St._ I, p. 61, 95, Marquardt, _Staatsverwaltung_, II,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]p. 582.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][28] For a thanksgiving after the expedition of Tiberius into Armenia
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]cf. Dio LIV, 9. Cf. also Cic. _Phil._ XIV, 11, 29. For two other
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]instances, cf. Mommsen, _R. G._, appendix, pp. 161-178.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][29] Not an incredible number. Thanksgivings were offered in Julius
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Cæsar’s time of fifteen, twenty, forty and fifty days. Cf. Drumann III,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]609, No. 84. Fifty days were decreed for the victories of Hirtius,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Pansa and Octavian in 711.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][30] The only names traceable are those of Alexander and Cleopatra, the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]children of Cleopatra and Alexander brother of Jamblichus, King of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Emesenes. Cf. Dio LI, 2, 21. Prop. 2, 1, 33, tells of “Kings with their
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]necks surrounded with golden chains,” in the triumph of Aug. 14, 725.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][31] The emperors assumed the consulship only irregularly and for
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]short periods. Their taking of the “tribunitial power” was not through
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]a regular election to the tribuneship, as was the case with the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]consulship, for Augustus as a patrician was ineligible; but it was the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]assumption of a power equal to that of the tribunes. This made the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]emperors sacrosanct, gave them the initiative and the veto, and well
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]subserved the fiction of their being the representatives and champions
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the people. For discussions of this power cf. Merivale, _Hist. of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Rom._ C. XXXI; Mommsen, _Röm. St._ II, p. 759, 771-777, 833-845.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Succeeding emperors, down to 268 A. D., dated their accession from
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the day of assuming the tribunitial power. The wording is peculiar in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]this sentence. May it not have been that Augustus expected his heir
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]or executors to fill in the exact dates at the time of his death, as
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]suggested in the introduction?
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][32] Dio, LIV, 1, writes: “In the following year (732) the Tiber
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]again overflowed; statues in the Pantheon were struck by lightning,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]so that the spear was knocked out of the hand of Augustus. Pestilence
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]was so violent in all Italy that year that there was no one to till
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the fields; and I think the same was the case in foreign lands. The
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Romans thought that this plague and famine had come upon them, because
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]they had not made Augustus consul that year; they wished to name him
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]dictator, and with great show of violence compelled the senate, shut
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]up in the curia, to decree this; threatening to burn them unless they
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]did it. So the senate approached Augustus with the twenty-four fasces
[ΕΚΤΟΣ](insignia of dictatorship, the consul having only twelve), and begged
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]him to accept the dictatorship and the administration of the food
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]supply. He did indeed undertake the latter charge, and ordered that
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]duumvirs, who had held the praetorship five years before, should be
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]yearly appointed to have charge of the distribution of grain, but would
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]by no means accept the dictatorship. When neither by words nor prayers
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]he could move the people, he tore his garments. For he justly wished to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]avoid the jealousy and hatred of that name, since moreover, he already
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]held a dignity and power superior to that of the dictatorship.” Vell.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]II, 89, 5, says: “The dictatorship which the people persistently thrust
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]upon him, he as constantly repelled.”
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The dictatorship had fallen into disuse after 552, and was revived,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]irregularly, by Sulla in 672. Cæsar made it the basis of his power,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]being made perpetual dictator shortly before his death. After that
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]event, on motion of Antony, the office was abolished.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][33] In Chap. 15, Augustus states that in 731 he twelve times
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]distributed grain at his own expense. This assumption of the grain
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]administration in 732 was not strictly a charity. The extract from
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Dio under Note 69, gives some of the details. It is probable that from
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]this time the tribute in kind was turned into the _fiscus_, or
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]imperial treasury, instead of into the _ærarium_, or treasury of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the senate, as heretofore. This new task of the imperial government
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]involved not merely the gratuitous distribution of grain to the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]ordinary Roman citizens (after 752 even to senators and knights), but
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]also the providing of a sufficient supply of grain for all purchasers
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]at a minimum price, often below the market value. It appears that grain
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]tickets “tessaræ frumentariæ” were distributed to the citizens entitled
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to free grain, and then, to assist the vast multitude of strangers,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]freedmen, and _attachés_ of the great houses, money tickets,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]“tessaræ nummariæ” were given out. Cf. Mommsen, _Röm. St._, II,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]992.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][34] Vell. II, 89; Suet. _Aug._ 26; Dio, LIV, 10. Dio’s statement
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]that Augustus in 735 accepted the consular power (differing from the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]consulship as the tribunitial power from the tribuneship. Cf. Note
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]31, Chap. 4.) for life, cannot be correct in face of the other two
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]authorities cited, who corroborate Augustus here. Chapter 8 tells of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]two special assumptions of the consular power for the taking of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]second and third census.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][35] Before the restoration of the text of this inscription, in this
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]case depending entirely upon the remains at Apollonia, it used to be
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]taught that Augustus accepted the formal superintendence of laws and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]morals. And there seemed to be good ground for such belief. Horace,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c., 740 in _Carm. IV_, 5, v. 22, says, “Morality and law have
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]subdued foul wrong;” and in _Ep._, II, 1, v. 1, “Since thou hast
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]protected Italy with arms, adorned her with morality, and improved her
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]with laws.” Ovid wrote, _Tristia_, II, 233: “The city wearies
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]thee with the care of laws and morals, which thou desirest should be
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]like thy own.” Suet. _Aug._ 27, says: “He accepted the control of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]laws and morals for life, as he had the tribunitial power; and in the
