[ΕΚΤΟΣ]exercise of this control, altho’ without the honor of the censorship,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]he yet thrice took the census of the people, the first and third times
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]with a colleague, the second time alone.” Dio, LIV, 10, 30, says that
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in 735 and 742 Augustus accepted this office for periods of five years.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]But the inscription shows that Suetonius and Dio were wrong, and that a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]natural but incorrect inference had been drawn from the poets.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]This power was offered to Augustus three times; in 735, 736 and 743,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and as often refused. Why was it offered, and why refused? Cf. Dio,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]LIV, 10; Vell. II, 91, 92; Suet. _Aug._ 19. While Augustus was in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Asia in 735 M. Egnatius Rufus, who is painted as a sort of Catiline,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]tried to obtain the consulship, and even to supplant Augustus, and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]stirred up sedition in the attempt. This so alarmed the senate and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]people that they offered Augustus the plenary power of legislation and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]coercion. The repetition of the offer in 736 was from a similar cause.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The reason for that of 743 is unknown. The power thus offered was
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]analogous to the decemvirate, or the Sullan dictatorship. Cf. Mommsen,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Röm._, St., II, 686.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][36] This sentence answers the second question asked in the above Note.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]It was part of Augustus’ policy to seem to keep wholly within the lines
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the constitution. Hence his refusal to accept any extraordinary
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]office. Yet his tribunitial power was new and extraordinary. Tacitus’
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]comment is caustic, _Ann._, III, 56: “That specious title (the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]tribunitial power) importing nothing less than sovereign power, was
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]invented by Augustus at a time when the name of king or dictator
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]was not only unconstitutional but universally detested. And yet a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]new name was wanted to overtop the magistrates and the forms of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]constitution.”
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][37] Dio, LIV, 16, names three laws promulgated by Augustus in 736: one
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]took cognizance of bribery by candidates for office; a second dealt
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]with extravagance; and a third was for the encouragement of matrimony.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][38] ᵃ in 736 Agrippa was associated with Augustus for five years. Cf.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Dio, LIV, 12; Vell. II, 90; Tac. _Ann._ III, 56.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]ᵇ in 741 Agrippa again for five years. Cf. Dio, LIV, 12, 28.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]ᶜ in 748 Tiberius for five years. Cf. Dio, LV, 9; Vell. II, 99; Suet.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Tib._ 9, 10, 11.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]ᵈ in 757 Tiberius for ten years. Cf. Dio, LV, 13; Vell. II, 103; Tac.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Ann._, I, 3, 10.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]ᵉ in 766 Tiberius for an indefinite time. Cf.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Dio, LVI, 28.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][39] Suet. _Aug._ 27: “He administered the triumvirate for
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]organizing the commonwealth through ten years.” Cf. C. I. L. I, p. 461
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and p. 466. The first triumvirate lasted from Nov. 27, 711, to Dec. 31,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]716; the second from Jan. 1, 717, to Dec. 31, 721. But cf. c. 34, N. 1.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][40] Cf. Dio, LIII, 1. This title had been conferred upon the senior
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]senator who had served as censor. Its only privilege was the right
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of speaking first in debate. The honor had fallen into abeyance with
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the death of Catulus in 694. It is readily seen how the revival of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]such a title and of the right to express his views before any other
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]senator, gave Augustus a quasi-constitutional initiative in the senate.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Gradually the title dropped its second part, and “prince” began to have
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]something of its modern significance. Cf. Tacitus, _Ann._ III, 53,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]for Tiberius’ view of its meaning.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Augustus’ notation of time here, “through forty years,” is similar to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the “thirty-seventh year of the tribunitial power” in Chap. IV, or “the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]seventy-sixth year” of Chap. 36.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][41] He was made _pontifex_ in 706 by Julius Cæsar. Cf. Cic.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Phil._ V, 17, 46; Vell. II, 59. For his taking the office of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_pontifex maximus_ cf. c. 10, N. 3.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][42] The date of Augustus’ assumption of the augurate is discussed by
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Drumann, IV, 250. Coins are the chief witnesses, and their testimony is
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]confused. The date probably was 713 or 714.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][43] A coin of Augustus (Cohen, _Jul._ 60; _Aug._ 88) has
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_imp. Cæsar divi f. III vir iter. r. p. c. cos. iter. et tert
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]desig._, which fixes the time as between 717 and 720; it has also
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the tripod, the symbol of the quindecemvirate.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][44] We can say only that Augustus received this dignity before
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]738; for there is a coin of that year showing the _simpulum_,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the _lituus_ and the tripod, the symbols respectively of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]three foregoing offices, and the _patera_, or bowl, that of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]septemviral office. The four colleges thus associated are the chief
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]ones. Cf. Chap. 9.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][45] The name of Augustus is twice found in the _Acta Fratrum
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Arvalium_, once in May, 767, in recording a vote, and in Dec., 767,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in the record of the nomination of his successor.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][46] Tacitus says the Titian Sodality was instituted by Titus Tatius
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]for keeping up the Sabine ritual. Cf. _Ann._ I, 54. The record
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]here is all that is known of Augustus’ connection with it.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][47] The fetials had charge of the formalities in declaring war and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]peace. Dio L, 4, says that Augustus went through the old-fashioned
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]ceremonies in declaring war against Cleopatra.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]These three colleges had fallen into abeyance in the time of Cicero.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Augustus undoubtedly revived them. Cf. Suet. _Aug._ 31. Such
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]restoration, and religious conservatism in general, as even in the case
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of Domitian, marks the policy of the emperors for two hundred years,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and was one of their favorite methods of posing simply as restorers of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the good old times.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][48] In 725. The Saenian law, passed by the people in 724, authorized
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]this proceeding, and the senate’s decree followed. Hence the order,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]“people and senate.” Cf. Tac. _Ann._ XI, 25; Dio, LII, 42. An
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]earlier creation of patricians is assigned by Dio to the year 721.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]But he is probably mistaken, as Tacitus, in the passage just noted,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]says that Claudius was obliged to create more patricians, “because the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]number had declined even after being recruited by the dictator Cæsar
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]under the Cassian law, and by Augustus the _princeps_ under the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Saenian law.” Such a creation was not a right of the principate. Cæsar
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and Augustus did it by special authorization of people and senate.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Claudius did it in virtue of his censorship, and this status continued
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]till Domitian absorbed the censorship in the principate, and assumed
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the right as a permanent one.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][49] During most of the republican history the senate numbered,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]ideally, three hundred. In Cicero’s time it had over four hundred
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]members. Julius Cæsar raised it to about nine hundred. Suet.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Aug._, 35, says: “By two separate scrutinies he (Augustus)
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]reduced to their former number and splendor the senate, which had been
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]swamped by a disorderly crowd; for they were now more than a thousand,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and some of them very mean persons, who, after Cæsar’s death, had been
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]chosen by dint of interest and bribery, so that they had the name of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Orcini among the people.” They were also called Charonites, because
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]they owed their elevation to the last will of Cæsar, who had gone into
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Orcus to Charon. Dio, XL, 48, 63, tells of freedmen in the senate and,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]XLIII, 22, of a private soldier; Gell., XV, 4, of a muleteer, cf.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Juvenal, _Sat._ VII, 199.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Dio, LII, 42, cf. LIII, 1, tells of the first scrutiny, in 725-6. A
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]hint from Augustus was enough to cause the withdrawal first of sixty,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]then of one hundred and forty senators. He also tells, LIV, 13, 14,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of a further revision in 736, by which the number was brought down to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]six hundred. He assigns a third sifting to 743 (LIV, 35), and a fourth
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to 757 (LV, 13). Mommsen, however, is inclined to connect the three
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]revisions of Augustus with the censuses of 726, 746 and 767, and to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]regard those of 736 and 757 as extraordinary, and therefore not named
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]by Augustus, in his desire to appear entirely within constitutional
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]lines. Cf. Mommsen, _R. G._, p. 35.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][50] Suetonius evidently depends on this inscription when he says,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Aug._ 27: “Three times he took the census of the Roman people,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the first and third times with a colleague, the second time alone.”
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]This first census was in 725-6. Cf. Dio, LII, 42; LIII, 1; C. I. L.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]IX, 422, _imp. Cæsar VI, M. Agrippa II cos.; idem censoria potestate
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]lustrum fecerunt_.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The lustrum was strictly the expiatory offering made at the close
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the census. The census had not been taken for forty-one years.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The number of Roman citizens of military age in 684 had been given
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]as but 450,000. This census of 726 reported 4,063,000. Probably the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]vast apparent increase rose from the fact of the earlier enumeration
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]counting only such as presented themselves before the censors in the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]city, while at the later time the citizens throughout the empire were
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]counted. Clinton, _Fasti Hellenici_, III, 461, estimates a total
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]free citizenship of more than 17,000,000. The total population of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]empire at this time, including citizens, allies, slaves and freedmen,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]has been estimated at 85,000,000. Cf. Merivale, _Rom._ cc. XXX,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]XXXIX.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The Greek of the inscription here reads erroneously 4,603,000.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][51] In 746. The result, 4,233,000, shows a gain of 170,000.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][52] In 767. Just before the death of Augustus. Result, 4,937,000; gain
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]since 746, 704,000.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][53] Suetonius, _Aug._ 34, relates his endeavors to compel
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]matrimony. In Chap. 89, Suetonius writes: “In reading Greek or Latin
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]authors he paid particular attention to precepts and examples which
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]might be useful in public or private life. These he used to extract
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]verbatim, and give to his domestics, or send to the commanders of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]armies, the governors of the provinces, or the magistrates of the city,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]when any of them seemed to stand in need of admonition. He likewise
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]read whole books to the senate, and frequently made them known to the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]people by his edicts; such as the orations of Quintus Metellus ‘For
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the Encouragement of Marriage,’ and those of Rutilius ‘On the Style
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of Building;’ to show the people that he was not the first who had
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]promoted those objects, but that the ancients likewise had thought them
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]worthy of their attention.” Cf. Livy, _Ep._ LIX; Gell., I, 6.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][54] These games were first held in 726, and every fourth year
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]thereafter. The expression “every fifth year” counts the year of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]games as the fifth of the old series and also the first of the new.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The consuls, or rather the consul Agrippa, Augustus not holding games
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in his own honor, celebrated the games of 726, the pontifices those of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]730, the augurs those of 734, the quindecemvirs those of 738, and the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]septemvirs those of 742. Cf. c. 7, N. 6. These games are mentioned by
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Dio, LIII, 1, 2; LIV, 19; Pliny, _Hist. Nat._ VII, 48, 158; Suet.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Aug._ 44. They came to a close with the life of Augustus. We do
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]not hear of them in connection with any subsequent emperor. Vows for
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]his good health had a special fitness, for according to Suetonius,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Aug._ LXXXI, he was almost an invalid. “During his whole course
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of life he suffered at times dangerous fits of sickness. He was subject
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to fits of sickness at stated times every year, for about his birthday
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]he was commonly indisposed. In the beginning of spring he was attacked
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]with an inflammation of the midriff; and when the wind was southerly,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]with a cold in his head. By all these complaints his constitution was
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]so shattered that he could not readily bear heat or cold.”
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][55] Cf. Suet. _Aug._ 59 and 98; Hor. _Carm._ IV, 5, 33; Dio,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]LI, 19.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][56] Dio writes of the year 725, LI, 20: “When letters were brought
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]about Parthian affairs it was decreed that he should be named in the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]hymns exactly as were the gods.” Tiridates, a Parthian pretender,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]sought the aid of Augustus. Cf. Chap. 32, and Dio, LI, 18. Augustus
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]balanced Tiridates against Phraates, the legitimate monarch, who sent
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]an embassy, and gave his son to Rome as a hostage.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][57] In 718, when Lepidus had been overthrown, the tribunitial
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]power had been given to Octavian, as formerly to Julius, for life.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Inviolability of person was one of the privileges of the tribunate.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Cf. Oros. VI, 18, 34; Dio, XLIX, 15; LI, 18; LIII, 32. These two later
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]statements relating to the years 724 and 731, Mommsen thinks have to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]do, the former with the extension of the tribunitial power beyond the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]city, and the latter to the making it annual, as well as perpetual, so
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]that the years of the principate could be reckoned by it. Cf. Chap.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]4, note 31. Cf. also App. _B. C._ V, 132, and for a discussion of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the tribunitial power as an expression of the principate, cf. Mommsen,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Röm. St._ II, 833, ff.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Wölfflin, cf. textual note, suggests, to fill the gap confessedly left
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]by Mommsen’s emendation, a reading which would be translated “that my
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]person should be sacrosanct.”
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][58] Augustus here characteristically avoids the name of Lepidus.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The latter “in the confusion and tumult had seized the supreme
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]pontificate,” cf. Livy, _Ep._ CXVII, “by craft,” cf. Velleius II,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]63; “Antony transferred the election of the pontifex maximus from
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the people to the priests again, and through them initiated Lepidus,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]almost entirely neglecting the customs of the fathers.” Cf. Dio, XLIV,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]53. Lepidus dying in 741, cf. Dio, LIV, 27, Augustus entered upon the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]office Mar. 6, 742. Cf. C. I. L., I. p. 387. It was unlawful to deprive
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]a living man of this office, cf. App., _B. C._, V, 131.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][59] October 12, 735. In C. I. L. I. p. 404, is found an inscription
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of that date: _Feriae ex senatus consulto, quod eo die imp. Cæsar
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Augustus ex transmarinis provincis urbem intravit araq(ue) Fortunae
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]reduci constituta._ There are also gold and silver coins (Eckhel VI,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]100; Cohen, _Aug._ nos. 102-108) with the inscription, _Fortunae
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]reduci, Cæsari Augusto senatus populusque Romanus_, Dio, LIV, 10,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]tells that Augustus after having arranged matters in Sicily, Greece,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Asia and Syria, returned to Rome, and that many honors were decreed
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to him, but that he would accept none of them, “but that an altar
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]should be consecrated to Fortune the Restorer, that the day should be
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]accounted a feast day, and that it should be called the Augustalia.”
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The location near the Porta Capena was chosen, because it was through
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]that gate Augustus would enter the city, coming by the Appian Way from
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Brundisium. The altar was dedicated on Dec. 15, C. I. L. X, 8375. Cf.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Dio, LI, 19; App. _B. C._ II, 106.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][60] Dio, LIV, 10, relates that in this year there were great tumults
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in connection with the consular comitia, and no election was possible.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]In consequence of this the senate sent messengers to Augustus urging
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]him to deal with the trouble. Q. Lucretius, one of the delegates, was
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]named consul by Augustus on the spot where they met. It is Mommsen’s
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]idea (_R. G._, p. 48) that the story of Dio, and the statement of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Augustus relate to the same event, and that Augustus was not willing
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to admit that so late in his reign, such disturbances could be, and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]that he therefore conveys the impression that what was really an appeal
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]for aid was rather an embassy of honor. This Mommsen thinks quite in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]keeping with the general character and method of Augustus. Bormann, on
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the other hand (_Schr. Nach._, p. 29), sees no conflict in the two
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]accounts. He believes that Dio narrates truthfully enough an earlier
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]deputation sent to Augustus, possibly at Athens, some time before
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]his return, and that Lucretius was named consul there by Augustus.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Then, some time later, the deputation of honor, as recorded in the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]inscription, was sent into Campania.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][61] That this annual sacrifice was instituted July 4, 741, appears
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]from C. I. L., I, 395. _Feriae ex. s. c. quod eo die ara Pacis Augustae
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in campo Martio constituta est Nerone et Varo cos._ Cf. Fasti of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Præneste, Jan. 30, C. I. L., I, 313, for day of the actual dedication;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]also Ovid, _Fasti_ I, 709; Dio, LIV, 25.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]This altar was probably on the Flaminian Way by which Augustus returned
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]from Gaul.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][62] The exact conditions necessary for the closing of the temple,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]viz., “peace won by victories” were first made known in 1882 by this
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]perfected text of the _Res Gestæ_.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][63] Cf. Livy, I, 19; Varro, V, 165. The temple of Janus (or as the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Romans called it, Janus, without the word temple,) (cf. Latin text and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Livy, l. c., and Horace, Carm, IV, 15, 9,) had been closed first under
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Numa and again after the first Punic War.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][64] Augustus first closed it in 725, after Actium. Cf. Livy, l. c.;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Dio, LI, 20; Vell., II, 38; Victor, _De Viris Ill._, LXXIX, 6;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Plut. _De Fort. Rom._, 9; Oros., VI, 20, 8. C. I. L. I, p. 384,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]supplies the day, January 11. In 728 it was opened again, on account of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the war with the Cantabri. Cf. Dio, LIII, 26, Plutarch, l. c. A second
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]time it was closed in 729, cf. Dio, l. c.; Oros., VI, 21, 1. The time
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of its next opening cannot be determined; but in all probability it was
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]reopened that very year, on account of the Arabian campaign. Dio, LIV,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]36, records that in 744 the Senate decreed that it should be closed,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]but that a Dacian rebellion interfered. But Dio must be mistaken, for
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Drusus was then in the midst of his German campaign. But after the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]campaigns of Drusus and Tiberius in Germany, closed in 746, up to 753,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]when Gaius Cæsar started for Armenia, the temple might well have been
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]closed. Parts of Dio are lost here, which may have mentioned such
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]closing. The birth of Jesus Christ, 749, falls in this period of peace.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Cf. Milton’s _Nativity Hymn_. When it was opened for the third
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]time cannot be said. Tacitus says it was opened when Augustus was an
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]old man. But it can hardly have remained shut after the opening of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Armenian war in 753. Augustus was then sixty-two years old. That age
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]may possibly suit the expression of Tacitus. Horace _Ep._, II, 1,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]255, and _Carm._, IV, 15, 9, mentions the closing of the temple.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Suetonius, _Aug._ 22, says: “Janus Quirinus, which had been shut
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]twice only, from the era of the building of the city to his own time,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]he closed thrice in a much shorter period, having established universal
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]peace both by sea and land.” This is almost a literal transcript of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Res Gestæ_.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][65] Gaius and Lucius, the sons of Agrippa and Julia, the daughter of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Augustus, were born, the one in 734 (Dio, LIV, 8), the other in 737
[ΕΚΤΟΣ](Dio, LIV, 18) and were adopted by their grandfather immediately after
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the birth of the latter. Dio, LIV, 18, says: “Lucius and his brother
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Gaius, Augustus at once adopted and made heirs of the empire, without
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]waiting till they grew to manhood, in order that he might be the more
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]secure against conspiracies.” The will of Augustus (Suet. _Tib._
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]23), speaks much as this chapter does of the death of the two Cæsars:
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]“Since harsh fortune has snatched from me my sons, Gaius and Lucius,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]let Tiberius Cæsar be heir to two-thirds of my estate.” Suetonius,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Aug._ 26, says that Augustus took his twelfth and thirteenth
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]consulships, for the purpose of introducing these two boys into the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]forum.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][66] Dio, LV, 9, under the year 748 writes that these lads were wild
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and insolent and that the younger, then eleven years old, actually
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]proposed to the people to make Gaius consul. Augustus appeared very
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]angry at this, saying it would be a public calamity for the consulship
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to be borne by one of less age than that at which he himself had
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]assumed it, viz., twenty. Gaius was, however, designated consul in 749,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and Lucius in 752. Cf. Tac. _Ann._ I, 3; a coin of Rome has on
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]one side: _Cæsar Augustus, divi. f., pater patriæ_; on the other:
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_C. L. Cæsares, Augusti f., cos. desig., princ. juvent._ (Eckhel
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]VI, 171). This must have been struck between Feb. 5, 752, when Augustus
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]received the title _pater patriæ_, and January 1, 754, when Gaius
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]entered upon his actual consulship. Cf. C. I. L. III, n. 323, and VI,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]900.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Lucius died, Aug. 20, 755, and so did not reach the consulship to which
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]he had been elected. Gaius died in 757. Cf. Dio, LV, II; C. I. L. I. p.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]472.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][67] Cf. Dio, LV, 9; C. I. L. I, p. 286 and 565.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][68] Dio, LV, 12, says: “The bodies of Lucius and Gaius were carried
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to Rome by military tribunes, and the chief men of each city; and the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]golden (sic) shields and spears, which they had received from the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]knights when they assumed the _toga virilis_, were suspended in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the curia.”
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The title of _princeps juventutis_ is somewhat difficult to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]explain. The fact is attested by Zonaras, X, 35, and by an inscription
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]found near Viterbo (cf. Mommsen _R. G._, p. 53), which reads:
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_C. Cæsari Aug. f.d.n. pontif. cos. design. principi juventut_,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]“To Caius Cæsar, son of Augustus, nephew of the divine (Julius)
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]pontifex, consul designate, prince of the youth.” Mommsen sums up his
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]investigation of this (Cf. _R. G._ p. 54, ff.): the knights were
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]divided into _turmæ_, or troops, each officered by _seviri_,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]three _decurions_ and three _optios_ or adjutants. Gaius
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and Lucius were _decurions_ of the first _turma_, and their
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]title, “princes of the youth,” was a special one, and always thereafter
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]reserved for members of the imperial family. The title does not appear
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to have been official, or formally bestowed, but was given by common
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]consent of the knights.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][69] Cf. Suet. _Cæs._ LXXXIII: “He (Cæsar) bequeathed to the Roman
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]people his gardens near the Tiber, and three hundred sesterces to each
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]man.” Dio, XLIV, 35, is peculiar, saying: “Cæsar left to the people his
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]gardens on the Tiber, and to each man one hundred and twenty sesterces,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]as Augustus himself says, or as others say, three hundred sesterces
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]apiece.” May it be that Dio has reversed the facts here, and that it
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]was “others” who reported the smaller sum and Augustus the larger?
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Augustus is substantiated, or followed, by Plut.; _Ant._, XVI,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Brut._, XX; App. _B. C._, II, 143.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Three hundred sesterces equals about fifteen dollars. The date of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]this disbursement is 710: its amount, supposing the minimum number of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]receivers, 250,000, comes to $3,750,000.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][70] The second (and the seventh, cf. Note 76) donations belong to the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]year 725 and were connected with the triple triumph. Dio mentions the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]two together, LI, 21. Four hundred sesterces is about twenty dollars.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][71] The third donation was in 730, on the return of Augustus after
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]subduing the Cantabri. Dio, LIII, 28, says: “Augustus gave the people
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]a hundred denarii (four hundred sesterces) apiece, but forbade the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]distribution until his act should receive the sanction of the senate.”
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]It would seem to have been unlawful to give money to the people without
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the consent of the senate. Probably this was a measure of precaution
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]against demagogues.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The term _congiarium_, which is transferred rather than translated,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]means a gift, primarily of food or drink, and is derived from
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_congius_, a measure holding about three quarts, which was perhaps
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]originally brought to be filled with grain or oil, or the like.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][72] Cf. c. 5 and Note 33. The date was 731.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][73] The fifth distribution was in 742. We learn from Dio, LIV, 29,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]that in that year Agrippa died, leaving to the Roman people his gardens
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and bath, and that Augustus, as his executor, not only turned over
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]these properties, but made a donation besides, as if it had been so
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]willed by Agrippa. Cf. C. I. L., I. p. 472.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][74] As c. 8 furnishes a basis for estimating the total population of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the empire, so here we have a guide to the number of people in the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]city. Merivale, _History of the Romans_, c. XL, gives 700,000 as
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the limit; Bunsen, 1,300,000; Gibbon, c. XXXI, 1,200,000.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][75] Sixty denarii is about twelve dollars. This donation of 749, and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the last one mentioned in this chapter, of 752, have been connected
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]with the introduction in those years of Gaius and Lucius Cæsar, into
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the forum. Cf. c. 14. The amounts are the same in the two cases, and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]they vary from the sum given at other times.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][76] Up to this point the donations have been enumerated in order of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]time. But here, between the largesses to citizens in 749 and 752 is
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]introduced one given to veterans in 725. Why this break in the order?
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Mommsen, _R. G._ p. 2 and 59, thinks that a first draft of this
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]inscription was prepared about 750. In this draft Augustus first
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]mentioned all his gifts to the city people; and at the end placed the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]one gift to the soldiers. Then, when in 767, the document was brought
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]down to date, this later gift to the people was placed last, instead
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of being interpolated after the civil donation of 749 and before the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]military one of 725. But his reasoning has not convinced other scholars.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][77] Cf. Dio, LV, 10.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][78] Augustus omits any mention of his bounty to discharged soldiers.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Cf. Dio, XLVI, 46; XLIX, 14; LV, 6; Appian, V, 129. The total of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]donations in this list is 619,800,000 sesterces = about $30,990,000.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][79] Cf. c. 3; Dio, LI, 3, 4; Suet. _Aug._ 17. The last writer
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]says that there was a mutiny at Brundisium in a detachment sent there
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]immediately after Actium, and that they demanded reward and discharge.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Augustus was forced to come from Samos to settle the trouble. This was
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in 724. There were 120,000 veterans to be provided for. Cf. c. 15.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]600,000,000 sesterces was the compensation for the lands given to these
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]men, an average of 5000 sesterces ($250) for each holding. But not
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]all Italian proprietors were reimbursed. The Italians who had favored
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Antony were simply dispossessed. To some other Italians were given
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]lands at Dyracchium and Philippi. His expenditure for land in Italy was
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]$30,000,000. As to colonies outside of Italy, Dio, LIV, 23, tells of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]many settlements in Gallia (Narbonensis) and Iberia in 739. Eusebius
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]notes colonies at Berytus in Syria, and Patræ in Achaia, as founded in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]739. Cf. _Chron._ ad. a. Abr. 2001; C. I. L. III, p. 95.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][80] The dates are 747, 748, 750, 751 and 752. The amount is
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]$20,000,000. It was in 741 (Dio, LIV, 25) that Augustus determined upon
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]a gift in money as a substitute for the assignments of land customary
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]up to that time. Why such payments began only in 747 is a matter of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]conjecture; also why they ceased after 752. Probably because the years
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]742-746 were occupied with the German and Pannonian wars of Tiberius
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and Drusus, and either there were no discharges, or else no money to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]spare from the expenses of war. Again in 753 troubles began in the East.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][81] Only two of these occasions can be traced. Dio, LIII, 2, mentions
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]one. He says that in 726, when it was determined to exhibit games in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]honor of Actium, Augustus replenished the empty treasury for that
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]purpose. And there is a coin of c. 738 with the inscription: _Senatus
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]populusque Romanus imperatori Cæsari quod viæ munitæ sunt ex ea pecunia
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]quam is ad ærarium detulit._ Eckhel VI, 105.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Up to 726 the treasury was in charge of the quæstors. Thence to 731 two
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]exprætors, after that year two prætors presided over it, up to the time
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of Claudius. Cf. Tac. _Ann._ XIII, 29; Dio, LIII, 2 and 32; Suet.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Aug._ 36. The sum mentioned here is $7,500,000. In the Greek τρίς
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]has evidently been omitted before χειλίας.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][82] This was in 759. In 741 (Dio, LIV, 25) Augustus had fixed the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]term of service at twelve years for the prætorians and sixteen for
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the legionaries. The gift to the former upon discharge was also
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]larger. In 758 the terms of service were lengthened to sixteen and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]twenty years. Cf. Dio, LV, 23. In LV, 25, Dio writes of this year 759:
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]“Augustus contributed, in his own name and in that of Tiberius, money
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]for that treasury which is called the military.” The sum so given was
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]$8,500,000. Tributary states and kings also assisted. But income could
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]not keep pace with expenses. The old tax of a twentieth on bequests,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]except when the heir was a very near relative, or very poor, was
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]revived, much to the discontent of the Roman people. Cf. Dio, LV, 25. Other
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]taxes were devised, such as that of one _per cent_ on sales. Cf.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Tac. _Ann._ I, 78. On sales of slaves two _per cent_ was
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]exacted. Cf. Dio, LV, 31.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]A glance at the military establishment of Augustus may help to some
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]idea of its vast expense. Mommsen discusses the matter in detail (_R.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]G._ pp. 68-76). Augustus seems to have left at his death a standing
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]army of twenty-five legions. Each legion approximated seven thousand
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]men, giving a total of 175,000 soldiers. His legions were numbered from
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]one to twenty-two. The number twenty-five is accounted for as follows:
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the seventeenth, eighteenth and nineteenth had been exterminated
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]under the leadership of Varus. But there were three legions, one in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Africa, one in Syria and one in Cyrenaica, bearing the title third,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and the fourth, fifth, sixth and tenth were each double. After Actium,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Augustus disbanded the legions numbered above twelve (cf. his colonies
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of veterans at this time, numbering 120,000 men, c. XV). But by reason
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the repetitions above alluded to, the legions bearing the numbers
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]up to twelve, really amounted to eighteen. These duplications may have
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]risen from the absorption into Augustus' army of legions bearing the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]same numbers from the forces of Lepidus and later from those of Antony.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]In 759, eight new legions, the thirteenth to the twentieth, seem to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]have been enrolled, in view of the German and Pannonian wars. This made
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]twenty six. Three were lost with Varus, and their numbers, seventeen,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]eighteen and nineteen, seem never to have been restored to the list.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]To offset this loss in a measure, two new legions, the twenty-first
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and twenty-second were levied. Thus the twenty-five remaining at the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]death of Augustus are accounted for. Such an establishment was
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]enormously and increasingly expensive. Pliny, _Hist. Nat._, VII,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]45.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][83] This form of benefaction began in 736. It is a little remarkable
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]that Augustus should not mention the exact years of its continuance,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]its amount, or the beneficiaries, while he does name the minimum number
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of men who received aid from time to time. Perhaps he did not go into
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]details because these gifts concerned the provincials and would be of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]slight interest to the city people for whose reading the inscription
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]was intended. In 742, “when Asia was in need of aid on account of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]earthquakes, he paid the year’s tribute of the province out of his own
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]means.” Dio, LIV, 30.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]His supplying grain as well as money rose from the fact that taxes
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]were imposed both in kind and in money. Cf. Tac. _Ann._ IV, 6;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Agr._ XIX and XXXI; C. I. Gr. 4957, 47. These passages all speak
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of taxes both in money and in produce. As to the method of levy,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Hyginus is interesting (_De Lim._ p. 205). “The tax on agriculture
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]is arranged in many ways. In some provinces the harvest is chargeable
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]with a certain proportion, here a fifth, there a seventh, elsewhere a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]cash payment, and for this purpose certain values are determined for
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the fields by an estimation of the soil; as in Pannonia there is arable
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the first class, of the second, meadows, mast-bearing woods, common
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]woods, pastures: upon all these the tax is laid by the single acre,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]according to the fertility of the soil.” This was in the time of Trajan.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][84] The structures detailed here and in cc. 20 and 21, fall into three
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]classes. First, those of c. 19, being either new buildings in place of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]ruined ones, or else entirely new ones, both classes on soil already
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]consecrated; second, those of c. 20, being repairs of public works;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]third, public works upon soil given by himself, as noted in the first
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]part of c. 21.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Augustus does not mention structures which he erected in the name of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]others, as the portico of Octavia, (different from the one below, Note
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]90), the portico of Livia, cf. Dio, XLIX, 43 and LIV, 23. He also omits
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the temple of Concord dedicated by Tiberius in 763 (C. I. L. I. p.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]384), though he paid for it.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The order of the works is chronological for the most part.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][85] This was the Curia Julia, begun in 712. Cf. Dio XLVII, 19; XLIV,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]5; XLV, 17. It was dedicated in 725 after Actium. Cf. Dio LI, 22. Here
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the senate met. Its location was near the forum.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][86] A shrine of Minerva Chalcidica.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][87] Begun after the Sicilian victories in 718. Cf. Dio XLIX, 15; Vell.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]II, 81, dedicated Oct. 9, 726. Cf. Dio, LIII, 1; C. I. L. I, p. 403.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Suet. _Aug._ 29, says: “He reared a temple of Apollo in that part
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of his estate on the Palatine which the haruspices declared was desired
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]by the god because it had been struck by lightning; he attached to it a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]portico and a Greek and Latin library.”
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][88] An altar was placed at once on the spot in the forum where the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]body of Julius Cæsar was cremated. In 712 the senate decreed that a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]temple should be built there.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][89] Dionysius (I, 32), observes that the ancient condition of this
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]place (originally a grotto near the Palatine, sacred to Pan) had
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]been so changed as to be hardly recognizable. This was by reason of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the changes made in his time, which nearly coincided with that of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Augustus. Cf. C. I. L. VI, 912, 6, 9, and 841. Its precise location is
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]undetermined.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][90] Festus, _De Verb. Sig._ L. 13, writes: “There were two
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Octavian porticoes, the one built near the theatre of Marcellus by
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Octavia, the sister of Augustus, the other close to the theatre of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Pompey, built by Cn. Octavius, son of Cnæus, who was curule aedile,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]prætor, consul (589) decemvir for the sacred rites, and celebrated
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]a naval triumph for a victory over King Perseus. It was the latter
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]which, after its destruction by fire, Cæsar Augustus rebuilt.” Its
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]reconstruction was in 721. Cf. Dio, XLIX, 43, who, however, confounds
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]this Octavian portico with the other built some years after in the name
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of Augustus’ sister, Octavia.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][91] The Pulvinar was the place of honor from which the imperial family
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]witnessed the games. Cf. Suet. _Aug._ 45; _Claud._ 4. This
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]restoration followed the burning of the Circus Maximus in 723. Cf. Dio,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]L, 10.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][92] A temple attributed to Romulus, in ruins in the time of Augustus,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]till restored by him on the suggestion of Atticus. Cf. Nepos,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Atticus_, 20; Livy, IV, 20. The temple was probably restored in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]723.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][93] Suetonius, _Aug._ 29, writes: “He dedicated the temple to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Jupiter the Thunderer, in acknowledgment of his escape from a great
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]danger in his Cantabrian expedition; when, as he was traveling by
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]night, his litter was struck by lightning, which killed the slave who
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]carried the torch before him.” This expedition was in 728-729, and the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]temple was dedicated Sept. 1, 732. Cf. Dio, LIV, 4; C. I. L. I, 400.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][94] This was dedicated in 738, on the Quirinal. Cf. Dio, LIV, 19.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][95] These three temples have more than an accidental collocation.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Just as the Tarpeian mount and the Quirinal hill had their triple
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]divinities, so had the Aventine. Cf. Varro (_De Lin._) V, 158. The
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]temple of Juno is ascribed to the time of Camillus, and is said to have
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]been built for the Veientines. The date of the other two is unknown, as
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]is that of this restoration by Augustus.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][96] Also of unknown origin, location and restoration, other than as
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]mentioned here.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][97] Dionysius, I, 68, describes the old temple, not the restoration by
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Augustus of which we have only this statement.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][98] The original temple was dedicated in 563, in the Circus Maximus.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Cf. Livy, XXXVI, 36. Burned in 738. Cf. Dio, LIV, 19.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][99] The original temple was burned in 756. Cf. Val. Max. I, 8, 11;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Dio, LV, 12; Suet. _Aug._ 57.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][100] The Capitol means the temple of Jupiter Capitolinus.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][101] Frontinus, _De Aq._ c. 125, speaks of a decree of the Senate
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in the year 743 “concerning the putting in order of the streams,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]conduits and arches of the Julian, Marcian, Appian, Tepulan and Aniene
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]waters, which Augustus has promised the Senate that he will repair at
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]his own expense.” Aqueducts were repaired in 749-750. Cf. C. I. L.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]VI, 1244. C. I. L. VI, 1249, gives _Iul. Tep. Mar.; imp. Cæsar divi
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]f. Augustus ex s. c.; XXV; ped. CCXL_. C. I. L. VI, 1243, records
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the repairs of the Marcian aqueduct. Frontinus, _op. cit._, 12,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]gives some details of the doubled supply of this source, and says the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]new spring had to be conducted eight hundred feet to join the older
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]fountain.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][102] Julius Cæsar dedicated this forum Sept. 24 or 25, 708. Cf. Dio,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]XLIII, 22; App. _B. C._, III, 28; C. I. L. I, p. 402 and 397.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Pliny, _Hist. Nat._, XXXV, 12, 156, mentions its completion by
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Augustus.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Augustus uses the word _profligata_ here for “unfinished,” a use
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]which was common enough but not elegant, and is severely criticised by
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Gellius, XV, 5. The word really means wretched rather than unfinished.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]That Augustus was not a purist this inscription testifies, and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Suetonius also tells us, _Aug._, 87 and 88, how peculiar he was in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]diction and orthography.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The basilica which was unfinished at the death of Augustus he refrains
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]from naming while it was not yet dedicated. But we know from Suetonius,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Aug._ 29, and Dio, LVI, 27, that it was built in honor of his
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]grandchildren, Gaius and Lucius.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][103] There is abundant testimony to this architectural activity. Cf.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Suet. _Aug._ 29 and 30; Dio, LIII, 2; LVI, 40; Livy IV, 20; Ovid,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Fasti_, II, 59; Hor. _Carm._, III, 6. Nor was this the zeal
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of a mere archæologist and architect. The emperor was anxious for a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]revival of religious observance, as a conservative force in his new
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]organization of the state.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][104] It is remarkable that Augustus should say he “_constructed_”
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the Flaminian Way, etc., for it was made nearly two hundred years
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]before this date, 727. Moreover, the whole chapter is given up to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]an account of reconstructions, and of course it is meant that he
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_repaired_ the road and the bridges in question. The Latin
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]verb is wanting and is restored from the Greek, ἐπόησα, which is
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]unmistakable,—“I made.” Mommsen does not comment on the incorrectness
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of this statement, but Wölfflin regards the Greek verb as a blunder
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the stone-cutter at Ancyra, and thinks there was no verb at all at
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the end of this chapter, but that the mason by mistake took the last
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]word of the preceding chapter which is ἐπόησα. A substitution of ἐπόησα
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]for the proper verb seems more likely, as it seems improbable that the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]sentence would end without a verb.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]These repairs are attested by an inscription on an arch at Ariminum,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]thus restored by Bormann: Cf. C. I. L. XI, 365.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    SENATUS POPULUS_Q ue romanus_
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  _imp. cæsari divi f. augusto imp. sept._
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  COS. SEPT. DESIGNAT. OCTAVOM _Via flamin_ IA _et reliquei_S
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]  CELEBERRIMEIS ITALIÆ VIEIS CONSILIO _et sumptib_ US _eius mu_NITEIS.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Cf. also Suet. _Aug._ 30; Dio, LIII, 22. Other roads of Italy were
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]repaired by those who obtained triumphs; of which more were celebrated
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]from 726 to 728 than at any other epoch.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][105] Cf. Suet. _Aug._ 29. Its construction was vowed in 712 and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]it was dedicated in 752. Cf. C. I. L. I, p. 393, May 12. In c. 35,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Augustus mentions the quadriga dedicated to him in this forum.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][106] This theatre was begun by Julius Cæsar. Augustus completed it
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in honor of Marcellus, who died in 731. It was dedicated May 4, 743.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Cf. Pliny, _Hist. Nat._, VIII, 17, 65. Dio, LIV, 36, assigns its
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]dedication to 741.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][107] Suetonius, _Aug._, 30, says that on one occasion Augustus
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]deposited in the _cella_ of Jupiter Capitolinus sixteen thousand
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]pounds of gold (= $3,200,000) and gems and pearls of the value of fifty
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]million sesterces (= $2,500,000). But such statements are fabulous, in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]view of Augustus’ own statement that the total of his gifts of this
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]kind was only one hundred million sesterces (= $5,000,000).
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][108] In earlier times it had been customary for cities affected by a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]victory to give crowns of gold to the triumphing _imperator_. This
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]grew into an abuse and was forbidden by law, unless the gift preceded
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the decree for the triumph. Later, the value of the crown was commuted
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]for cash, and it came to be a frequent means of extortion on the part
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of provincial governors. To L. Antonius crowns of gold were given by
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]each of the thirty-five Roman tribes in 713. Cf. Dio, XLVIII, 4. The
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]amount named here, thirty-five thousand pounds of gold, would appear to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]have been from the thirty-five tribes. On the general subject, _aurum
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]coronarium_, cf. Marquardt, _Staatsverwaltung_, II, p. 285.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][109] The sons of Augustus were Gaius, adopted in 737, died in 757;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Lucius, adopted at the same time, died in 755; Agrippa Postumus,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]adopted in 757, exiled in 760. These were the sons of Agrippa and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Julia. On the death of Gaius in 757, Augustus adopted Tiberius. With
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]him Germanicus, nephew and adopted son of Tiberius, and Drusus,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Tiberius’ own son, became the legal grandchildren of Augustus. None of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]these could celebrate games in his own name after adoption, as they had
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]no property rights, but were absolutely dependent on the head of their
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]house, according to the _patria potestas_ of the Roman law. See
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]this very plainly set forth in Suetonius, _Tib._ 15: “After his
[ΕΚΤΟΣ](Tiberius’) adoption he never again acted as master of a family, nor
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]exercised in the smallest degree the rights which he had lost by it.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]For he neither disposed of anything in the way of gift, nor manumitted
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]a slave; nor so much as received an estate left him by will, or any
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]legacy, without reckoning it as a part of his _peculium_, or
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]property held under his father.” Tiberius was forty-six years old when
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]he was adopted.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Seven of these exhibitions can be traced. 1. In 725, on the dedication
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the temple of the Divine Julius. Dio, LI, 22. 2. In 726, in honor
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the victory of Actium. Dio, LIII, 1. 3. In 738, in accordance with
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]a decree of the senate. This was in the name of Tiberius and Drusus.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Dio, LIV, 19. 4. In 742, at the Quinquatria held March 19-23, in honor
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of Minerva. This was in the name of Gaius and Lucius. Dio, LIV, 28, 29.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]5. In 747; funeral games in honor of Agrippa. Dio, LV, 8. 6. In 752,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]at the dedication of the temple of Mars. Vell. II, 100. 7. In 759, in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]honor of Drusus, in the name of his sons Germanicus and Claudius. Dio,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]LV, 27; Pliny, _Hist. Nat._, II, 26, 96; VIII, 2, 4. Possibly the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]eighth occasion may be found in Suetonius, _Aug._, 43.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][110] Cf. Dio, LIII, 1; Suet. _Aug._, 43. Wooden seats were
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]erected in the Campus Martius for gymnastic contests in 726. Whether
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Germanicus or Drusus is the grandson mentioned here is unknown.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][111] These were the lesser games of the circus and theatres, given
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]ordinarily by magistrates holding the lower offices, which Augustus
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]never filled. He took upon himself the care and expense where the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]proper magistrates were absent or too poor. Cf. Dio, XLV, 6; C. I. L.,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]I, p. 397.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][112] The charge of the Secular Games, celebrated supposedly once in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]a century, though in reality oftener, fell to the quindecemvirs. Cf.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Eckhel, VI. 102, for a coin with _imp. Cæsar Augustus lud. saec. XV
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]S. F._ This was in 737. Cf. also C. I. L., I, p. 442. The college
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]evidently gave the presidency to Augustus and Agrippa, since it was
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]very convenient that these two members of the sacred body also held the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]tribunitial power, and so the games came into the charge of the two
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]greatest men of the state in a perfectly natural way. Cf. C. I. L., IX,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]p. 29, No. 262, for confirmation of Agrippa’s membership in the college
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of quindecemvirs.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][113] These games were celebrated on August 1. Dio, LX, 5, and LVI, 46,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]tells of their being annual, and in charge of the consuls after the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]death of Augustus. They began in 752. This passage is one of the few
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]where both the Latin and Greek are incapable of restoration.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][114] Cf. Suet. _Aug._ 43. Some of these occasions were: in 743 in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]connection with the dedication of the theatre of Marcellus. Cf. Dio,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]LIV, 26. Here six hundred beasts were killed, and the tiger was shown
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]for the first time. Cf. Pliny, _Hist. Nat._, VIII, 17, 65. In 752,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]two hundred and sixty lions and thirty-six crocodiles were killed. Cf.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Dio, LV, 10. In 765, in the games given by Germanicus, two hundred
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]lions were killed. Cf. Dio, LVI, 27.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Augustus says “amphitheatres,” though there was but one such structure.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]He may have regarded it as being two theatres joined at their straight
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]side and facing each other.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][115] Velleius II, 100, writes: “The divine Augustus in the year when
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]he was consul with Gallus Caninius (752) sated the minds and the eyes
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the Roman people at the dedication of the temple of Mars with the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]most magnificent gladiatorial shows and naval battles.” Dio, LV, 10,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]says that traces of the excavation could be seen in his time (c. 200 A.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]D.), and that the fight represented a battle of Athenians and Persians,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in which the former were victorious. Cf. Suet. _Aug._ 43; Ovid,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Ars Am._ I, 171.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Claudius gave a similar exhibition on the Fucine Lake, but with a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]hundred triremes and quadriremes, and a force of nineteen thousand
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]men, “as once Augustus did in a pond by the Tiber, but with lighter
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]vessels and a smaller force.” Cf. Tac. _Ann._ XII, 56; Suet.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Claud._, 21; Dio, LX, 33.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][116] Another instance of avoidance of the name of an enemy while
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]distinctly referring to him. Antony had stripped various temples at
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Samos, Ephesus, Pergamos, and Rhœteum, all in the province of Asia,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and had given the spoils to Cleopatra. Dio, LI, 17, says that great
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]numbers of such things were found in her palace when Alexandria was
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]captured. Pliny, _Hist. Nat._, XXXIV, 8, 58, says: “He (Myro) made
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]an Apollo, which was taken away by the triumvir Antony, but restored
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to the Ephesians by the divine Augustus.” Strabo, XIII, 1, 30, writes
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of Rhœteum: “Cæsar Augustus gave back to the Rhœtians the shrine and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]statue of Ajax which Antony had taken away and given to Egypt. He did
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the like for other cities. For Antony took away the finest votive
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]offerings from the most famous shrines for the gratification of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Egyptian woman, but Augustus restored them.” Ib. XIV, 1, 14, writes of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the temple of Hera, at Samos: “Antony took away three colossal sitting
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]statues on one base, but Augustus Cæsar restored two of them, Athene
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and Heracles, to the same base; the Zeus, however, he placed upon the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Capitol.”
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][117] Suetonius, _Aug._, 52, says these gifts took the form of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]tripods. Cf. Dio, LIII, 22; LII, 35; LIV, 35.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][118] The allusion is to Sextus Pompeius, whose fleets, manned largely
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]by slaves, cut off the grain ships on their way to Rome. Again Augustus
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]avoids the name of an opponent. Cf. Vell., II, 73, who thinks it
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]remarkable that a son of the great Pompey, who had freed the sea from
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]pirates, should himself defile it with piratical crimes. Florus, IV, 8,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]reflects the same sentiment. App. _B. C._, V, 77, 80, says that
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]captured pirates under torture confessed that Sextus Pompeius was the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]instigator of their crimes. When the peace of Misenum was made, Sextus
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Pompeius stipulated for the freedom of the slaves who had fought under
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]him. It was after the overthrow of Pompey, in 718, that the slaves were
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]returned. Dio, XLIX, 12, adds that slaves whose masters did not claim
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]them were returned to their several cities, there to be crucified. Cf.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]App. _B. C._, V, 131; Oros. VI, 18.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][119] This was in 722, just before the breaking out of hostilities
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]between Antony and Octavian. Cf. Dio, L, 6; Suet., _Aug._ 17.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][120] Cf. c. 8, Note 49. There were a thousand senators at this time.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Augustus, in his statement, probably means that seven hundred of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]thousand then in the senate were on his side, not merely seven hundred
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]who then or later were senators.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The number of consulars, eighty-three, is quite consistent with the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]facts, as is shown in a careful analysis of the _Fasti Consulares_
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]for the period by Mommsen. _R. G._, p. 100.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The priests referred to were probably members of the four great
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]colleges and the Arval brotherhood. Cf. c. 7, notes 40-45.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][121] This statement is borne out by what we otherwise know. Taking
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the provinces in order we find: First, the German frontier is pushed
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]forward from the Rhine to the Elbe. Cf. Suet. _Aug._ 21. Second,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in Illyricum and Macedonia he had erected the new provinces of Pannonia
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and Moesia. Third, in Asia Minor he did not extend the older limits of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Bithynia, but out of the kingdom of Amyntas, he made the new province
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of Galatia and later added Paphlagonia to it. Fourth, in Africa,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Augustus rather narrowed than extended the empire by his partition with
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Juba in 729. But a number of Roman proconsuls won laurels there.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][122] Here the record is of commotions quelled within the recognized
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]limits of the empire. In Spain there was the Cantabrian war from 727 to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]735. In Gaul, G. Carrinas had subdued the Morini, and triumphed, July
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]14, 726; and M. Messala had suppressed the Aquitani, triumphing Sept.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]25, 727. Cf. Suet. _Aug._, 20, 21.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The German campaigns extending at intervals over the years from 742
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to the very end of Augustus’ reign it is needless to detail. This
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]reference to the pacification of Germany has been the subject of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]much dispute. Mommsen in two places (_R. G._, p. VI, and 48),
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]uses the word “crafty” (_callidus_) of Augustus, referring to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]his alleged glozing over of unsatisfactory events. Hirschfeld goes
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]further, and in connection with the present passage accuses Augustus
[ΕΚΤΟΣ](_Wiener Studien_, V, 117) of a “masterly concealment and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]whitewashing (übertünchung) of all that could hurt his reputation.”
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]This charge is made because Augustus omits all mention of the disaster
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]under Varus. Against this charge Johannes Schmidt defends Augustus,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ](_Philologus_, XLV, p. 394, ff.). The contest between Schmidt and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Hirschfeld is based really upon opposing views of the purpose of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Res Gestae_. Schmidt believed it to be an epitaph. In this there
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]would be no place for anything save the fortunate events of a life.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]If _nil de mortuis nisi bonum_ be wise, Augustus might well have
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]adapted the adage to his own case and said, _nil de me morituro nisi
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]bonum_. But Hirschfeld insists that the _Res Gestae_ constitute
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]not an epitaph, but “an account of his administration,” and therefore
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]contends that the omission of the German disaster was not in good
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]faith. To this, Schmidt answers that Augustus had nothing to gain by
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]such concealment—indeed that concealment of so notorious a disaster
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]would be absurd. And in the text itself he finds a recognition of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]real state of affairs, inasmuch as Augustus expressly distinguishes
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Germany from the provinces, Gallic and Spanish, and while claiming
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]it for Rome, does not assert that it belongs to her as do organized
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]provinces. Schmidt also says that _pacavi_, “I pacified” does
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]not necessarily imply that Germany continued in a state of peace. It
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]may well enough cover the fact that there was temporary success. But
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]this is hair-splitting. The character of the _Res Gestae_ must
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]be always had in mind. Cf. Introduction. Its deliverances were _ad
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]populum_ and they constituted an epitaph.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][123] Suetonius, _Aug._ 21, says: “He waged war upon no people without
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]just and necessary causes.” The present Torbia near Monaco, derives its
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]name from a _Tropæa Augusti_, “Trophy of Augustus,” some fragments of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]which still exist.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The inscription has been preserved by Pliny, _Hist. Nat._, III, 20,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]136: _imp. Cæsari divi f. Augusto pontifice maxumo imp. XIIII tribunic.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]potestate XVII s. p. q. R. quod ejus ductu auspiciisque gentes Alpinæ
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]omnes quæ a mari supero ad inferum pertinebant sub imperium p. R.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]sunt redactæ_—“the Roman senate and people to Cæsar ... Augustus ...
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]because under his leadership and auspices all the Alpine nations, from
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the upper to the lower sea have been brought into subjection to the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Roman empire.” Then follows an enumeration of forty-six peoples. Pliny
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]adds, “the Cottian states were not annexed because they had not been
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]hostile;” and an arch at Segusio was placed in honor of Augustus, and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]on it are the names of fourteen states, six being repetitions from the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Torbia monument. Cf. C. I. L. V, 7817 and 7231.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The campaigns here referred to are: First, of Varro Murena against the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Salassi in 729. Cf. Strabo, IV, 6, 7, p. 205; Dio, LIII, 25; Livy,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Epit._, CXXXV; Cass. _ad. ann._ 729; Suet. _Aug._
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]21. Second, of Publius Silius against the Vennones and Camunni in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]738. Cf. Dio, LIV, 20. Third, of Tiberius and Drusus against the Ræti
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and Vindelici in 739. Cf. Suet. _Aug._ 21. Fourth, against the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Ligurians of the Maritime Alps in 740. Cf. Dio, LIV, 24. Finally these
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]regions were formed into the province of Rætia in 747-748.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][124] This naval expedition was connected with the German campaign of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Tiberius in 758. Cf. Vell. II, 106; Pliny, _Hist. Nat._, II, 67,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]167.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][125] Strabo, VII, 2, 1, describes an embassy of the Cimbri asking for
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]“peace and amnesty.” They dwelt in the end of Jutland. Cf. Ptolemy,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]II, 10. Below them were the Charudes, whom the mason at Ancyra makes
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Charydes, and the Greek translator, thinking of the fable, transforms
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]into Chalybes, living just south of the Cimbri. Cf. Ptolemy, ii, 11,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]12. The Semnones were between the Elbe and the Oder.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][126] When the Egyptian garrisons were weakened on account of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Arabian expedition, Queen Candace took advantage of it and captured
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]a number of towns in Upper Egypt. These the præfect, C. Petronius,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]re-took, and inflicted severe punishment upon the Æthiopians. This took
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]place 730-732. Cf. Strabo, XVII, I, 54; Dio, LIV, 5; Pliny, _Hist.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Nat._, VI, 29, 181, 182.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]In 1896 Capt. Lyons, R. E., found, at Philæ, an inscription in Latin,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Greek and hieroglyphics, of which Prof. Mahaffy gives this translation:
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]“Gaius Cornelius, son of Cnaeus Gallus, a Roman knight, appointed
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]first prefect, after the kings were conquered by Cæsar, son of Divus,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of Alexandria and Egypt—who conquered the revolt of the Thebaid in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]fifteen days, having won two pitched battles, together with the capture
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the leaders of his opponents, having taken five cities, some by
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]assault, some by siege, viz., Boresis, Coptos, Ceramice, Diospolis
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the Great, Ombos (?); having slain the leaders of these revolts, and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]having brought his army beyond the cataract of the Nile to a point
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]whither neither the Roman people nor the Kings of Egypt had yet carried
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]their standards, a military district impassable before his day; having
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]subdued, to the common terror of all the kings, all the Thebaid, which
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]was not subject to the kings, and having received the ambassadors of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the Ethiopians at Philæ, and guest-friendship from their king (and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]received their king under his protection) and having appointed him
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]tyrant of the 30-_schoeni_ district of Lower Ethiopia—makes this
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]thank-offering to the Dii Patrii, and to the Nile, who aided him in his
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]deeds.” _London Athenæum_, March 14, 1896, and _Sitzungsberichte
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]d. kgl. Pr. Akad. d. Wiss. zu Berlin_, 1896, I, pp. 469-480.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][127] The Arabian campaign, under C. Aelius Gallus was probably in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]729-730. Cf. Dio, LIII, 29; Hor. _Carm._ I, 29, 35; Strabo, XVI,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]4, 22, 24. Pliny, _Hist. Nat._, VI, 28, 159, 160,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][128] Egypt was made an integral part of the empire after Actium
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and the death of Cleopatra, in 724. Its connection with the empire
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]was peculiar. W. T. Arnold, _Roman Provincial Administration_,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]p. 113, says: “The government of Egypt was in many points wholly
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]exceptional. Julius Cæsar had deliberately abstained from making
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]it a province of the country (cf. Suet., _Jul._ 35); and when
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Augustus added it to the empire he subjected it to an altogether
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]exceptional treatment. The country was his private property, or rather
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the Emperor’s private property; it passed as a matter of course, that
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]is, from emperor to emperor. Augustus appointed a præfect to represent
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]him in the province, just as in earlier times the urban prætors had
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]sent prefects to represent them in the municipalities of Italy. This
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]præfect was of equestrian, and not of the highest equestrian rank (Tac.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Ann._, XII, 60; II, 59; _Hist._ I. 11); no senators were
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]admitted into the province; and the greatest jealousy was shown of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]smallest interference with it. The reasons for the special jealousy
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of Egypt shown by Augustus and his successors were partly the great
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]defensibility of the country (in case of insurrection—ED.), partly
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]its immense importance as the granary of Rome. 'It was an accepted
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]principle with our fathers,’ says Pliny, 'that our city could not
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]possibly be fed and maintained without the resources of Egypt.’” For
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]a fuller treatment cf. Marquardt, _Röm. Staatsverwaltung_, I,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]282-298.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][129] Armenia Major had been raised to greatness by Tigranes I
[ΕΚΤΟΣ](658-699) who had been a formidable ally of Mithridates. Pompey finally
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]subdued him, 688. Henceforth Armenia was in a subject condition.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Tigranes was succeeded by his son Artavasdes. In 718, when Antony
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]attacked the Parthians, this king sided with him against Phraates of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Parthia, and another Artavasdes, king of Media. Cf. Dio, XLIX, 25.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]But presently the two Artavasdes changed relations, the king of Armenia
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]passing to the Parthian side and he of Media joining Antony. Cf. Plut.,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Ant._, 52; Dio, XLIX, 33, 44. Antony captured Artavasdes of Armenia
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and gave him over to Cleopatra, who killed him in 721. His kingdom
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]was assigned to Antony’s son Alexander to whom was betrothed Jotape
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]daughter of Artavasdes of Media. The Armenians made Artaxes, son of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the late Artavasdes, their king. When Octavian overcame Antony he did
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]not befriend all the Oriental enemies of the latter, but for purposes
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of his own set up a rival to Phraates of Parthia in Tiridates. Cf. c.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]32. And, angered at the Armenians, who had dealt harshly with certain
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Romans in that kingdom, he held as hostages the brothers of king
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Artaxes, and set Artavasdes of Media over Armenia Minor as a check upon
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Artaxes. Cf. Dio, LI, 16; LIV, 9. In 734 Augustus went to the East to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]arrange affairs there. A campaign against Artaxes was planned, but he
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]was assassinated. Cf. Dio, LIV, 9; Tac., _Ann._, II, 3; Vell., II, 94,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]122; Suet. _Aug._, 21; Jos., _Ant._, XV, 4, 3; Eckhel, VI, 98. At this
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]point the action of Augustus, recorded here in the _Res Gestæ_, takes
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]place. Augustus follows the example of Pompey, who, in dealing with
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Armenia in 688 had contented himself with making the Armenian king
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]accept his royalty as a gift from Rome. Cf. Cic. _pro Sext._ 27. The
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]affair was conducted by Tiberius, not yet adopted. Cf. Suet. _Tib._,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]9; Vell., II, 122. Henceforth Armenia was regarded as part of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]empire, though its native sovereigns were continued. Cf. Vell., II, 94,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]122: “Armenia restored to the control of the Roman people;” “Armenia
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]retaken.” “The Medes likewise were subjected.” Cf. c. 33.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][130] The reign of Tigranes was brief. The Parthians winning some
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]success against Rome, stirred up Armenia. Cf. Tac. _Ann._, II,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]3; Vell., II, 100. They favored the children of Tigranes, Tigranes
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]III and Erato. A Roman faction set up his younger brother Artavasdes.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Cf. Tacitus l. c. The suppression of the disorder was enjoined upon
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Tiberius. But at this juncture, 748, he went into retirement at Rhodes.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Cf. Dio, LV, 9. Artavasdes died and the young Tigranes courted the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]aid of Rome, but was soon killed, probably by Parthian means, and his
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]sister Erato abdicated. Cf. fragments of Dio, cited by Mommsen, _R.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]G._, p. 113, and Dio, LV, 10. Tacitus confirms the delivery of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Armenia to Ariobarzanes by Gaius. Cf. _Ann._, II, 3; and Dio, LV,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]10. The Parthian faction did not accept him, and it was in a contest
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]over him that Gaius received a wound, of which he died, Feb. 21, 757.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Cf. C. I. L. I, p. 472. For the succession of Artavasdes, cf. Dio,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]LV, 10. The Tigranes IV, next mentioned “of the royal house of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Armenians” was a grandson of Herod the Great, of Judea, on the one
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]side, and of Archelaus, King of Cappadocia, and probably an Armenian
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]princess on the other. Cf. Tac. _Ann._ VI, 40; XIV, 26; Jos.,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Ant._ XVIII, 5, 4; _Wars_, I, 28, 1.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][131] For Sicily and Sardinia, cf. c. 25 and notes.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]By the treaty of Brundisium, Antony had received Macedonia, Achaia,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Asia, Pontus, Bithynia, Cilicia, Cyprus, Syria, Crete, Cyrenaica. The
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]five last named he had given over to foreign kings. As to Asia and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Bithynia, Dio, XLIX, 41 and Plut. _Ant._ 54, are in conflict.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]But the _Res Gestæ_ tends to confirm the latter. Lycaonia and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Pamphylia were taken from the province of Cilicia and given to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Amyntas, King of Galatia. Cf. Dio, XLIX, 32. He extended Egypt again
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]by restoring to it Cyprus. Cf. Dio, XLIX, 32, 41; Plut. l. c.; Strabo,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]XIV, 6, 6: he granted to Cleopatra and Cæsarion, her son by Julius
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Cæsar, the coast land of Syria, Tyre and Sidon excepted, cf. Jos.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Ant._ XV, 4, 1; _Wars_, I, 18, 5; also Coele-Syria, cf. Jos.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Ant._ XV, 3, 8; Plut. l. c.; Ituraea, Judaea and Arabia Nabataea,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]cf. Dio, XLIX, 32; Jos. _Ant._ XV, 4, 1; 5, 3; _Wars_, I, 18,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]5; 20, 3; parts of Cilicia, cf. Strabo, XIV, 5, 3; 5, 6: and perhaps
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Crete also, cf. Dio, XLIX, 32: and Cyrenaica, cf. Plut. l. c. To his
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]younger son Ptolemy Philadelphus he gave Syria, and part of Cilicia,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]cf. Dio, XLIX, 41; Plut. l. c.: for the elder, Alexander he planned a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]kingdom made up of Armenia, Media and Parthia, cf. Livy, _Epit._
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]CXXXI; Plutarch, l. c. These alienations of Roman territory were made
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the occasion of Octavian’s attack upon Antony. Cf. Dio, L, 1; Plut. l.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]c.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][132] Mommsen believes that Augustus founded only military colonies.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Zumpt thinks otherwise. Cf. _Comment Epig._, I, 362.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][133] Known colonies of Augustus are: In Africa, Carthage, cf. C. I. L.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]VIII, p. 133; Dio, LII, 43; App. _Pun._ CXXXVI. In Sicily, Panhormus,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Thermes, Tyndaris, cf. Dio, LIV, 7; Pliny, _Hist. Nat._, III, 8, 88;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]89; 90. Marquardt, _Röm. Staatsverwaltung_ I, 246, names seven colonies
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of Augustus in Sicily. In Macedonia, Dyrrachium, Philippi, cf. Dio,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]LI, 4. Cassandrea, cf. Pliny, _Hist. Nat._, IV, 10. In Hither Spain,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Cæsaraugusta, cf. coin in Eckhel I, 37, which also gives the numbers
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the legions whose veterans were colonized here: _leg. IV_, _leg.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]VI_, _leg. X_. Marquardt _op. cit._, I, 256, names six colonies of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Augustus here. In Farther Spain, Emerita, cf. Eckhel I, 12, and 19,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_leg. V_, _X_; Marquardt, _op. cit._, I, 257. In Achaia, Patrae, cf.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]C. I. L. III, p. 95, _leg. X_, _XII_. In Asia, Alexandrea of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Troad, cf. Pliny, _Hist. Nat._, V, 30. In Syria, Berytus, cf. Eckhel
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]III, 356, _leg. V_, _VIII_; Heliopolis, cf. Eckhel, III, 334. In
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Gallia Narbonensis, Reii and Aquae Sextiae, cf. Herzog, _Gall. Narb.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]inscr._ n. 113, 356. In Pisidia, Antioch, cf. Eckhel III, 18; Cremna,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]cf. Eckhel III, 20; Olbasa, cf. Eckhel, III, 20; Parlais, cf. Ramsay,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Bull. de Corr. Hell._, VII, p. 318.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]No colonies are assigned to Sardinia, the three Gauls and two
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Germanies, Raetia, Noricum, Bithynia, Pontus, Galatia, Galatian Pontus,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Paphlagonia, part of Phrygia, Lycaonia, Isauria, Cilicia, Cyprus,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Crete, Egypt, Cyrenaica. As for parts of the empire under subject
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]kings, such as Thrace, Cappadocia, Mauretania, no account is taken of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]them, though there were certainly colonies in Mauretania, at Cartenna
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and Tupusuctu. Cf. Pliny, _Hist. Nat._, V, 2, 20; C. I. L., VIII,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]8857.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][134] Cf. an article by Mommsen, _Hermes_, XVIII, 161 ff. on the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]“Colonies of Italy from Sulla to Vespasian.”
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]When Augustus wrote, Italy was separated from Illyricum by the river
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Arsia. Yet Illyricum was not counted by him as a province. It had
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]colonies at Emona, Iader, Salona, and possibly at Epidaurus and Narona.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Cf. C. I. L., III, pp. 489, 374, 304, 287, 291. Mommsen thinks this
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]omission was intended by Augustus; that he had been able to satisfy
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]some of his veterans, to whom Italian farms had been promised, with
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]lands over the Italian border in Illyricum, and because he could not
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]call it a province, nor yet a part of Italy, he eludes the difficulty
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]by omitting the Illyrian colonies.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The names of the twenty-eight Italian colonies are somewhat difficult
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to establish. Several perplexing questions rise in the attempt. What
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the colonies founded by Antony and Octavian as triumvirs? Were they
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Antoniæ Juliæ, or some Juliæ and others Antoniæ? If the former were
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]true and they dropped the name Antoniæ, the result would be far more
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]than twenty-eight Julian and Augustan colonies. The second probability
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]is more likely, and that the colonies Antoniæ simply dropped their name
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]after Actium.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]A third difficulty rises in the case of the enlargement of old
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]colonies and their resettlement, as, _e. g._, of Minturnæ.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Cf. Hyginus, _De Lim._, p. 177. Mommsen gives a list which
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]nearly meets the statement of Augustus. 1. Ariminum, _Augusta_;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]2. Ateste; 3. _Augusta_ Prætoria; 4. _Julia Augusta_
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Taurinorum; 5. Beneventum, _Julia Augusta_; 6. Bononia; 7.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Brixia, _Augusta_; 8. Capua, _Julia Augusta_; 9. Castrum
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]novum Etruriæ, _Julia_; 10. Concordia, _Julia_; 11. Cumæ
[ΕΚΤΟΣ](?) _Julia_; 12. Dertona, _Julia_; 13. Fanum Fortunæ,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Julia_; 14. Falerio; 15. Hispellum, _Julia_; 16. Lucus
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Feroniæ, _Julia_; 17. Minturnæ; 18. Nola, _Augusta_; 19.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Parentium, _Julia_; 20. Parma, _Julia Augusta_; 21. Pisae,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Julia_; 22. Pisaurum, _Julia_; 23. Pola, _Julia_;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]24. Sæna (?), _Julia_; 25. Sora, _Julia_; 26. Suessa,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Julia_; 27. Sutrium, _Julia_; 28. Tuder, _Julia_;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]29, Venafrum, _Julia Augusta_. Cf. Marquardt, _Röm.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Staatsverwaltung_, I, 118-132.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][135] Of standards recovered in Spain and Gaul we have no further
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]knowledge. It may be that in the Cantabrian war of 728, 729, some such
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]thing took place.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Appian, _Illyr._ XII, XXV, XXVIII, narrates the capture of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]standards by the Dalmatians from Gabinius in 706, and their restoration
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to Augustus in 721. These were then placed in the Octavian portico; and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]probably later transferred to the temple of Mars.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][136] The standards had been lost by Crassus and Antony. Cf. Justin,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]XLII, 5, 11; Livy, _Epit._, CXLI; Suetonius, _Aug._ 21;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Vell., II, 91; Vergil, _Æn._ VII, 606; Horace, _Carm._, I,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]12, 56; III, 5, 4; Dio, LIII, 33; LIV, 8; Cass. _Chron._ ad. 734;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Oros., VI, 21; Florus IV, 12; Eutropius, VII, 9. One detachment of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Antonius’ army, under L. Decidius Saxa, was exterminated in 714, and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]another in 718 under Oppius Statianus. Cf. Livy, _Ep._ CXXI; Dio,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]XLVIII, 24.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Tiberius received the standards from the Parthians in 734. Cf. Dio,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]LIV, 8, etc.; Suet. _Tib._ 9. Eckhel, VI, 95, shows a coin with
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]a Parthian on bended knee presenting a standard to Augustus. Cf. also
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Horace, _Epis._, I, 12, 27; Oros., VI, 21, 29; and c. 32 of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]inscription.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]There were two temples of Mars Ultor, a smaller one on the Capitoline,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and a larger in the forum, dedicated in 752. The standards were removed
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to the larger temple. Cf. Dio, LV, 10; Horace, _Carm._, IV, 5, 16;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Epis._, I, 18, 56; Propertius, III, 10, 3; Ovid, _Trist._
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]II, 295; _Fasti_, V, 549; VI, 459.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][137] Augustus himself had fought the Pannonians in 719, 720. Cf. Dio,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]XLIX, 36-38. The campaigns of Tiberius were from 742 to 745. Cf. Vell.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]II, 96; Dio, LIV, 31, 34; LV, 2; Suet. _Tib._, 9.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][138] This statement varies somewhat from Dio, L, 24, who says Augustus
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]reached the Danube in 720, and from Suetonius, _Tib._ 16, who
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]assigns the complete subjection of the district to 759.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][139] The Dacians had become organized and strong in the latter years
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the Roman republic. Cf. Justin. XXXII, 3; Jordanis, _Get._, XI,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]67; Strabo, XVI, 2, 39; VII, 3, 5; 11; Suet. _Aug._, 44. Julius Cæsar
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]was about to proceed against them when he died. Cf. Suet. _Jul._, 44;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Aug._, 8; App. _B. C._, II, 110; III, 25, 37; _Illyr._, 13; Vell., II.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]59; Livy, _Epit._, CXVII. In 719 Augustus began his Illyrican campaign
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]by occupying Segesta on the Save, whence he threatened the Dacians
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and Bastarnæ. Cf. App. _Illyr._, 22, 23. Antony is responsible for
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the statement that Augustus sought to secure the goodwill of Cotiso,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]king of the Getæ (Dacians), by giving him his daughter and by himself
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]marrying a daughter of Cotiso. Cf. Suetonius, _Aug._, 63. Cotiso
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]refused the alliance and joined the party of Antony. Cf. Dio, L, 6; LI,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]22. Antony’s story as to the proposed marriages is hardly credible,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and may have been invented by him to offset his own alliance with
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Cleopatra. During the struggle between Antony and Octavian, an invasion
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the Dacians was the constant dread of Italy. Cf. Vergil, _Georg._,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]II, 497; Hor. _Sat._, II, 6, 53; _Carm._, III, 6, 13. When Antony was
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]overthrown M. Crassus undertook the suppression of the Dacians, and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]triumphed, July 4, 727. Cf. Dio, LI, 23; Tab. Triumph. But Dacian
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]incursions were still frequent. Dio records one in 738, cf. LIV, 20;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and one in 744, cf. LIV, 36. Probably it was in this latter incursion
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]that the defeat here alluded to was met by them. Finally an army was
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]sent against them under Lentulus, in 759. Cf. Dio, LV, 30; Strabo, VII,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]12 and 13; Suet. _Aug._, 21; Florus, IV, 12, 19, 20; Tac. _Ann._, IV,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]44.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][140] Cf. Suet. _Aug._, 21; Flor. IV, 12, 62; _Oros._, VI,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]21, 19, says that deputies of Indians and Scythians came to Augustus
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]at Tarracona in 728 or 729; Dio, LIV, 9, that deputies from India came
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to him at Samos in 734. Strabo gives the name of the Indian king as
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Porus. Cf. XV., 1, 4 and 73. Cf. also Ver. _Georg._, II, 170;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Aen._, VI, 794; VIII, 705; Hor. _Carm._, I, 12, 56; _Carm.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Saec._, 55, 56; _Carm._, IV, 14, 41.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][141] For a general statement, cf. Suetonius, _Aug._ 21. For the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Scythians, cf. Note 140 , above. For the Bastarnæ, cf. Livy, _Ep._
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]CXXXIV; Dio, LI, 23, 24. For the Sarmatæ, cf. Flor. l. c.; Strabo,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]II, 5, 30; Tac. _Ann._, VI, 33; Pliny, _Hist. Nat._, II,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]108, 246; VI, 7, 19; VI, 5, 16; VI, 13, 40. Vergil refers to them as
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Gelones. Cf. _Aen._, VIII, 725. Cf. also Hor. _Carm._, II, 9;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]III, 8, 23. For the Albani and Iberi, cf. Dio, XLIX, 24. For the Medes,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]cf. c. 27 and notes.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][142] For Phraates and Tiridates, cf. Justin, XLII, 5; Dio, LI, 18.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Tiridates had supplanted Phraates and in turn was driven out by him. He
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]then, in 724, came to Augustus for aid. But the latter was anxious to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]regain the lost standards from Parthia, and simply played off Tiridates
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]against Phraates by setting him over Syria. Dio, in the passage cited,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]makes mention of a son of Phraates who was captured by Tiridates and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]given up to Augustus. This was possibly the Phraates here mentioned,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]though there are difficulties in the way of this explanation. For
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Augustus implies the voluntary coming of a reigning king, not the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]delivery of an abducted prince. We know that in 731 Tiridates was in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Rome asking that Parthia be assigned to him, and that at the same time
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Phraates sent an embassy begging the restitution of his son. Cf. Dio,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]LIII, 33. Augustus laid the matter before the senate, and by their
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]advice restored the prince in exchange for the standards, but did not
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]yield to the plea of Tiridates.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][143] Cf. c. 27.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][144] A people east of the Tigris, and west of Media Atropatane.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Nothing is known of Artaxares. For the Adiabeni and their kingdom, cf.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Strabo, XVI, 1, 19; Tac. _Ann._, XII, 13; Josephus, _Ant._,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]XX, 2, 1.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][145] Augustus several times was on the point of invading Britain. Cf.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Dio, XLIX, 38, for 720; LIII, 22, 25, for 727, 728. The poets have many
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]prophecies of victories in Britain. Cf. Ver. _Georg._, I, 30,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]written in 724; III, 25; Hor. _Epode_, VII, 7; _Carm._, I.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]35, 29, of the year 727, 728; _Carm._, III, 5; I, 21, 15; III, 4,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]33; IV, 14, 48. But nothing came of these plans. Cf. Strabo, IV, 5, 3,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]for embassies from Britain. Coins of Dumnobellaunus have been found.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Cf. J. Evans, _Coins of the Ancient Britons_ (London, 1864), p.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]198, and the following plate 4, Nos. 6-12.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][146] The great defeat of Lollius in 738 was by the Sicambri, joined
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]with the Usipites and Tencteri. Cf. Dio, LIV, 20; Vell., II, 97; Suet.,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Aug._, 23. There was a temporary peace. Cf. Horace, _Carm._,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]IV, 2. 36; 14, 51. They rebelled in 742, and were put down, first by
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Drusus and later by Tiberius. Cf. Dio, LIV, 32, 33, 36. In 746 they
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]were completely subjugated and removed into Gaul. Cf. Dio, LV, 6; Vell.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]II, 97; Suet., _Aug._, 21; _Tib._, 9; Tac. _Ann._, II,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]26; XII, 39; Strabo, VII, 1, 3. Probably the coming of Maelo was during
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]this surrender of 746.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][147] The Marcomani were a branch of the Suevi. Cf. Tac., _Germ._,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]XXXVIII; _Ann._, II, 44, 62.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][148] The four sons were Seraspedes, Rhodaspedes, Vonones and
