[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Phraates, with the wives of two of them and four children. Cf. Strabo,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]XVI, 1, 28; VI, 4, 2; Justin, XLII, 5, 11; Vell., II, 94; Tac.,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Ann._, II, 1; Oros., VI, 21, 29; Suet., _Aug._ 21, 43; Jos.,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Antiq._, XVIII, 2, 4. They were sent to be out of harm’s way
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]during troubles in Parthia, according to all but Josephus, who says
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]they were removed so as not to hinder the succession of Phraataces, an
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]illegitimate son. When Phraates died, Phraataces in vain asked Augustus
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]for the return of the princes. This was c. 750. Cf. Dio, fragments,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Ursin. 39. The two elder princes died in Rome. Cf. C. I. L., VI, 7799.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Vonones was sent back by Augustus. Cf. c. 33, Note 149; Phraates was
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]returned by Tiberius in 788. Cf. Tac., _Ann._, VI, 31; Dio, LVIII,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]16. Probably the princes were sent to Augustus in 744. Cf. Mommsen,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_R. G._, p. 141.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][149] The comment of Mommsen here seems too severe. He says: “The
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]writer magnifies his splendors beyond what is exact: for the Parthians
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and Medes asked Augustus, not so much to appoint kings for them, as
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to restore to them those to whom the kingdom had fallen by hereditary
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]right.” Such a criticism seems to overlook the force of the word
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_petitos_, as applied to _reges_: they got the kings they “asked for.”
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Phraataces was reigning in 754. Cf. Dio, LV, 10; Vell. II, 101. He was
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]succeeded by Orodes for a short time. Then came the choice of Vonones.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Cf. Jos. _Ant._ XVIII, 2, 4; Tac. _Ann._ II, 1. Josephus
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]gives no date. Tacitus implies 770. Augustus, however, returned
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Vonones, and the date must be much earlier, probably c. 760. A Parthian
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]embassy was in Rome between 757 and 759. Cf. Suet. _Tib._, 16.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Coins also show the name of Vonones in 761. Cf. Gardner, _Parthian
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Coinage_, p. 46. His reign was very brief. Cf. Tacitus and Josephus,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]ll. cc.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][150] Cf. c. 27.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][151] This chapter is possibly the most weighty in the whole
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]inscription, inasmuch as it sets forth the view of his policy which
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Augustus wished the world to hold. How far his statements in the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]opening and closing sentences represent his own actual notions of his
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]relations to the sovereign power in Rome is a matter of debate. For a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]full discussion Mommsen, _Röm. St._ II, p. 723, ff., may be read,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and Gardthausen, _Aug._ Iᵉʳ Th. IIᵉʳ Bd., pp. 485-540 and IIᵉʳ
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Th., pp. 277-299.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The question is: Did Augustus in any real sense restore the republic,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]or did he conceive of himself as monarch, but find it politic to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]suppress all outward marks of royalty? Was his chief concern to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]maintain the peace and prosperity of the Roman people, with as little
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]alteration as possible of the old constitutional forms, or was his
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]object the building up of power for his own sake? This is confessedly
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]one of the riddles of history. The best that can be done is to study
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]his actions, estimating their worth and tendency, and leaving the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]motives of the great statesman where he hid them,—locked in his own
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]bosom.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Undoubtedly, all through the _Res Gestæ_, as is pointed out in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the introduction, and as has been noticed from time to time in these
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]notes, one of his great aims is to represent himself as a conservative,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]moving within constitutional limits. Coins of the period emphasize the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]view set forth in the opening sentence of this chapter with regard to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the restoration of the republic. Cf. Eckhel, VI, 83: _imp. Cæsar
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]divi f. cos. VI, libertatis p. R. vindex_; “The imperator, Cæsar,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]son of the divine (Cæsar) consul for the sixth time, (726) restorer of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the freedom of the Roman people.” Cf. C. I. L. VI, 1527: “the whole
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]world pacified, the republic restored.” Also, C. I. L. I, p. 384; the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]date referred to is Jan. 13, 727: “The senate decreed that an oaken
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]crown should be fixed above the door of the imperator, Cæsar Augustus,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]because he restored the Roman republic.” Contemporary Roman writers
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]simply echo the views of Augustus. Cf. Ovid, _Fasti_, I, 589,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]for Jan. 13, 727, Velleius, II, 89, says: “When the civil wars were
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]finished in the twentieth year, (724) and the foreign wars brought to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]a close, peace was brought back, power restored to the laws, authority
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to the tribunals, majesty to the senate, the _imperium_ of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]magistrates reduced to its old time form, the original and ancient
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]form of the state restored.” Cf. Livy, _Epit._, CXXXIV. The
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Greek Strabo, also a contemporary, writes, XVII, 3, 25: “The country
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]committed to him the headship of her sovereignty, and made him lord of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]peace and war for life.” Later writers, even the Romans, are equally
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]free in their judgments. Dio, LII, I, says: “From this time (725) the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]affairs of Rome began to be in the control of one man (μοναρχεῖσθαι).”
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Cf. Suet. _Aug._, 28; Tac. _Ann._, III, 28. Dio’s account of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the conference in which Agrippa advises a real abdication by Augustus,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and Mæcenas urges a bold assumption of supreme power (LII, 1-40) is
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]regarded as fictitious.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The facts in the case are these: In 711 the Titian law gave the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]triumvirs a five years’ lease of power. In 716 this was renewed not
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]by formal legislation, but “by universal consent.” Cf. App., _B. C._ V, 95. This triumviral power Augustus wielded till his sixth
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]consulship, 726, though there was a pretence of its cessation in 721.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Cf. c. 7, N, 1, and Mommsen, _Röm. St._, II, 698. In this and the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]following years he divested himself gradually of one extraordinary
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]power after another. He could not at once fall back to the position
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of an ordinary magistrate. The armies, the laws, the provinces, the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]revenues had all been in his control. These he must gradually restore
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Cf. Dio, LII, 13; LIII, 4, 9, 10. In 726 he began his return to older
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]customs by alternating with Agrippa, his colleague, in the consulship,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in having the fasces borne before him by the lictors for a month. Cf.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Dio, LIII, 1. The restoration of the censorship was part of the same
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]programme. Dio, LIII, 2, says that by an edict he declared all the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]revolutionary and extraordinary acts of the triumviral period should
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]cease to be effective with the expiration of his sixth consulship
[ΕΚΤΟΣ](726). The inscription of Jan. 13, 727, above alluded to, C. I. L. I,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]p. 384, marks that date as that on which the business of restoring the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]provinces was finally given over to the senate.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]From this time on the senate divided the control of the provinces
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]with him. Augustus took the troublesome provinces and the frontier
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]ones, leaving to the senate the older and more peaceable. Over these
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]provinces he received a proconsular imperium for ten years, which was
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]renewed at the expiration of that term. In c. 7 he says that he found
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the tribunitial power a sufficient basis for all the measures which
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]he wished to put through. Now the proconsulship and tribuneship were
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]both ordinary and constitutional offices. Augustus’ occupancy of each
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]affords an illustration of the way in which he held ordinary offices in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]an extraordinary way. For by the old customs a proconsul must exercise
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]his _imperium_ in his province, and never at Rome. Augustus could
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]not be in ten provinces at once, and must be at Rome most of the time.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Hence a violation of the constitution was necessary. The tribuneship,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]instituted for the protection of plebeians could be held only by a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]plebeian. But Augustus was a patrician. For this reason he did not
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]take the tribuneship in the ordinary way, nor by the ordinary title,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]but designated himself as _tribunicia potestate_, “of tribunitial
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]authority.”
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The title _princeps_, “prince” is never used by Augustus as an
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]official designation in laws and inscriptions, but indicates simply his
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]primacy of rank and is so used throughout the _Res Gestæ_. Cf. cc.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]13, 30, 32.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][152] Cf. C. I. L. 1, p. 384; X. 8375; Livy, _Ep._, 134; Cass. ad.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]an. 727; Oros. VI, 20, 8; Vell. II, 91; Suet. _Aug._ 7; Dio, LIII,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]16.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][153] Cf. coins in Eckhel, VI, 88; Cohen, _Aug._ nos. 43-48, 50,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]207-212, 301, 341, 356, 385, 426, 476-8, 482. All these show either
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the crown or the laurels and many of them have both. With the crown is
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]generally _ob civis servatos_, “for preserving the citizens.” The
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]civic crown being the reward of any soldier who saved a citizen’s life,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Augustus was pre-eminently deemed worthy of it, because he had saved so
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]many by putting an end to the civil wars, and by his clemency. Cf. Dio,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]LIII, 16; Suet. _Claud._ 17; Sen. _De Clem._ I, 26, 5; Ovid,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Tr._ III, 1, 39, 41, 47; _Fasti_ IV, 953; III, 137; Val.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Max. II, 8, 7; Juv. VI, 52, 79; X, 65; XII, 91; Tac. _Ann._ XV, 71.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][154] No ancient writer mentions this shield, but a number of coins and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]inscriptions portray it. Cf. C. I. L. IX, 5811, wherein two Victories
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]carry a shield inscribed: “The senate and Roman people have given
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to Augustus a shield on account of his valor, clemency, justice and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]piety;” the very words of the _Res Gestæ_. For coins, cf. Eckhel,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]VI, 95, 103, 121; Cohen, _Aug._ nos. 50-53, 213-216, 253, 264-267,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]283, 286-297, 332. The Victory, which is frequently associated with the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]shield, probably indicates that the latter was placed by Augustus near
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the altar of Victory erected by him in the Curia Julia.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][155] Cf. Note 151.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][156] This title was given Feb. 5, 752. Cf. C. I. L. I, p. 386; II,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]No. 2107. As in the case of the title, prince of the youth, conferred
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]upon Gaius and Lucius, and of the continuance of his supreme power by
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]universal consent (cf. cc. 14 and 34), the appellation, father of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]fatherland, was given by general acclamation, leaving to the senate
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]only the formal ratification of the popular will. Suet. _Aug._ 58,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]expressly states this. Cf. also Ovid, _Fasti_, II, 128.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The Augustan Forum was dedicated this same year, 752. Cf. c. 21, Note.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]In all probability the quadriga had been in existence some time before
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]this, inasmuch as it appears on a coin of uncertain date with the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]inscription: “the senate and Roman people to Cæsar Augustus, parent and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]presever.” If the quadriga had been made at the time this inscription
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]was ordered, the coin would surely have borne the formal title, “father
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the fatherland,” not the designation, “parent.” Cf. Eckhel, VI, 113.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][157] The seventy-sixth year of Augustus began Sept. 23, 766. Chapter
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]8 mentions his third census, which was completed one hundred days
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]before his death, hence May 11, 767. The _Res Gestæ_ must have
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]been written, then, in the interval between this date and his start for
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Campania, on his last journey, as we know he left this document in the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]hands of the Vestal Virgins. Cf. Suet. _Aug._ 97.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][158] Equivalent to 2,400,000,000 sesterces, about $120,000,000. This
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]does not exactly correspond with the sum of the items mentioned in the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]_Res Gestæ_. These sum up 2,199,800,000 sesterces.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][159] A mere summary of c. 19, with a bit from c. 20, the only
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]principle of arrangement being to put temples first, and the rest
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]haphazard. The difference in the Greek and Latin is curious. No attempt
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]is made to reproduce _pulvinar_ in Greek, although in c. 19 it had
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]been rendered ναόν.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][160] A summary of c. 20.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][161] A summary of cc. 22, 23.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][162] For aid given to Naples, cf. Dio, LV, 10; to Venafrum, in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Campania, C. I. L. X, 4842.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][163] For aid to Paphos, cf. Dio, LIV, 23; to a number of towns in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Asia, Dio, LIV, 30; to Laodicea and Tralles, Strabo, XII, 8, 18; to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Thyatira and Chios, Suet. _Tib._ 8.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ][164] Cf. Suet. _Aug._ 41. The estate necessary to qualify a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]senator he raised from 800,000 sesterces to 1,200,000, and where
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]senators were worthy, though poor, he made up their fortunes to that
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]sum. Cf. Dio, LI, 17; LII, 19; LIII, 2; LIV, 17; LV, 13; LVI, 41.
